201401	71	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Item 4 is “Proposed Revisions to the Authorization for Foreign Currency Operations, the Foreign Currency Directive, and the Procedural Instructions with Respect to Foreign Currency Operations.”	44	4
201401	72	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	I think there were only technical amendments here, but let me ask Simon if he has anything to say about these two.	44	4
201401	73	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Thank you, Mr Chairman.	44	4
201401	74	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	I have a prepared text.	44	4
201401	75	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	I’ll try to get through it pretty quickly.	44	4
201401	76	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Okay.	44	4
201401	77	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	At its first meeting each year, the Committee reviews the authorizations you just spoke about.	44	4
201401	78	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	And, with regard to the domestic open market operations, I recommend that the Committee approve the authorization with one small wording change that would make the structure of paragraph 1A similar to the structure of paragraph 1B.	44	4
201401	79	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	In addition to this change, I’d like to update the Committee on two items related to the domestic authorization.	44	4
201401	80	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	First, as you know, the System Open Market Account (SOMA) contains a significant amount of agency debt and agency MBS, and it is conducting transactions in MBS.	44	4
201401	81	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As such, I recommend a continued suspension of the Guidelines for the Conduct of System Open Market Operations in Federal- Agency Issues.	44	4
201401	82	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Second, the current authorization codifies the Open Market Trading Desk’s ability to transact in agency MBS for the SOMA through agents such as asset managers.	44	4
201401	83	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	This year, we plan to remove this service from our agreements with the asset managers, which would allow for the removal of paragraph 3 from the domestic authorization next January.	44	4
201401	84	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	No Committee vote is needed related to these two items.	44	4
201401	85	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Turning to foreign currency operations:  The Desk conducts such operations under the terms of the Authorization for Foreign Currency Operations, the Foreign Currency Directive, and the Procedural Instructions with Respect to Foreign Currency Operations.	44	4
201401	86	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	I recommend that the Committee approve these documents with three sets of amendments.	44	4
201401	87	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Please note that the vote to reaffirm these documents will include approval of the System’s warehousing agreement with the Treasury.	44	4
201401	88	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The first amendment is to remove paragraph 8 in the foreign authorization, which discusses the transmission of pertinent information on System foreign currency operations to appropriate officials of the Treasury Department.	44	4
201401	89	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	I propose that this type of communication instead be governed by the Program for Security of FOMC Information, which currently governs the transmission of similar information.	44	4
201401	90	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The memo circulated ahead of the meeting titled “Proposed Amendments to FOMC Organizational Documents” detailed how the staff recommends that this be addressed in the Program for Security of FOMC Information.	44	4
201401	91	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The second set of proposed amendments pertains to the central bank swap arrangements.	44	4
201401	92	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	At the October 2013 meeting, the Committee approved standing facilities with the Bank of Canada, the Bank of England (BOE), the Bank of Japan, the ECB, and the Swiss National Bank.	44	4
201401	93	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As a result, new language in the three documents is intended to incorporate these liquidity swaps and, where appropriate, align the treatment of the liquidity swaps and that of the reciprocal swaps that were put in place with the central banks of Mexico and Canada as part of the North American Framework Agreement (NAFA).	44	4
201401	94	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	There are five specific changes I’d like to highlight related to the swap arrangements.	44	4
201401	95	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	First, I propose aligning the review and approval process for any changes in the terms of existing NAFA swap arrangements with those for the liquidity swap arrangements.	44	4
201401	96	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Under this proposal, changes in the terms of existing swaps would be referred for review and approval to the Chairman instead of the Committee.	44	4
201401	97	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The Chairman would keep the Committee informed of any changes in the terms, and the terms shall be consistent with the principles discussed with, and guidance provided by, the Committee.	44	4
201401	98	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	To enact this change, I propose moving the language in paragraph 2 of the Foreign Authorization on “changes in the terms of existing swap arrangements” to the Procedural Instructions with the addition of paragraph 1D.	44	4
201401	99	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	I also propose replacing the reference to “the proposed terms of any new arrangements” in paragraph 2 of the Foreign Authorization with broader language that states, “Any new swap arrangements shall be referred for review and approval to the Committee.”	44	4
201401	100	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Second, I propose eliminating references to the maximum term of any drawing under the NAFA swaps in the Foreign Authorization in light of the previous proposal to have the procedural instructions govern the terms of all swap drawings.	44	4
201401	101	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The swaps will remain “subject to annual review and approval by the Committee.”	44	4
201401	102	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	This affects paragraphs 1C and 2A of the foreign authorization.	44	4
201401	103	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Third, I would like to align the annual review process of the liquidity swaps with that of the NAFA swaps by subjecting the liquidity swaps to “annual review and approval” instead of just “annual review.”	44	4
201401	104	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	This also affects paragraph 2 of the Foreign Authorization.	44	4
201401	105	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	While small, this change will require an annual vote on the liquidity swaps.	44	4
201401	106	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	I plan to ask for this vote at the third or fourth FOMC meeting of the year along with the vote on the NAFA swaps.	44	4
201401	107	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	This is consistent with the approach Steve Kamin and I proposed in the October 21, 2013, memo on this topic.	44	4
201401	108	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Fourth, I would like to expand the current arrangement for consulting with the Committee prior to initial liquidity swap drawings.	44	4
201401	109	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Specifically, I propose that the Chairman or the Foreign Currency Subcommittee will consult with the Committee when possible.	44	4
201401	110	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The resolution states that just the Foreign Currency Subcommittee will consult with the Committee when possible.	44	4
201401	111	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	I address this change with the proposed addition of paragraph 2A in the Procedural Instructions.	44	4
201401	112	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Lastly, I would like to clarify that any changes in the terms of existing liquidity swap arrangements shall be referred for review and approval to the Chairman, consistent with my earlier proposal for handling changes in the terms of the NAFA swaps.	44	4
201401	113	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The resolution approved by the Committee in October specified the approval process for changes to the rates and fees only.	44	4
201401	114	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	I address this change with the proposed addition of paragraph 2B in the procedural instructions.	44	4
201401	115	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The third set of proposed amendments clarifies the link between the Procedural Instructions and the Foreign Authorization through additions to the wording in the new paragraph 3A.iii and paragraph 4 in the Procedural Instructions.	44	4
201401	116	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	I would also like to update the Committee on one item related to the foreign portfolio.	44	4
201401	117	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Paragraph 6 of the Foreign Authorization requires that all foreign operations “be reported promptly to the Foreign Currency Subcommittee and the Committee.”	44	4
201401	118	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The Desk performs a wide variety of tasks within its mandate to manage the foreign portfolio, and the reporting time varies by each specific operation, depending on the nature of each activity.	44	4
201401	119	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The memo we circulated ahead of the meeting titled “Request for Votes on Authorization for Desk Operations” included an appendix that clarifies the New York Fed’s Markets Group staff’s reporting practices related to operations conducted under the foreign authorization.	44	4

201401	145	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	I’m not sure the nuances there will be picked up by the marketplace, but, nonetheless, I’m fine with that.	92	9
201401	146	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	Regarding the second change, though, there remains considerable uncertainty both in the marketplace and around this table about what we mean by true maximum employment and what it actually is.	92	9
201401	147	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	And I think we struggle with understanding what it means and how it varies over time.	92	9
201401	148	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	I’m concerned that, unfortunately, over time, the markets and the public have come to think of what we report in the SEP as somehow our target as opposed to some information about our assessment, despite what we’ve said.	92	9
201401	149	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	We had a discussion about this when we first launched this statement—and maybe this is a topic for the Committee coming up—but I’d be inclined to try to drop the last two sentences of that paragraph altogether so that we don’t find ourselves having to change the unemployment numbers every time the SEP changes.	92	9
201401	150	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	We ought to think about doing that—at least dropping the last sentence even if we don’t drop the second-to-last sentence.	92	9
201401	151	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	So I’d like to put that on the table as something for discussion and for consideration by this Committee.	92	9

201401	160	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But I’m very glad to hear that a subcommittee will be formed to evaluate the statement in its third year of use.	92	9
201401	161	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In particular, I think that—and I mentioned this last time at our meeting—as time evolves and we get closer to maximum employment and inflation gets closer to target, financial-stability concerns are likely to play more of a role in our deliberations.	92	9
201401	162	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And financial stability is mentioned explicitly in the second paragraph as being a factor that we take into account when we think about policy.	92	9

201401	164	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So I think that is a potential opportunity for improvement that the subcommittee could take into account; that was the only comment I had.	92	9

201401	174	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Mr Chairman.	92	9
201401	175	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	I will again abstain from the vote on the adoption of this statement.	92	9
201401	176	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	On the one hand, I continue to believe that it doesn’t actually reflect a strong enough consensus among Committee members to permit a more effective communication of our policies to the public.	92	9
201401	177	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	On the other hand, I don’t think it’s done any particular harm, and, particularly as interpreted and explained by the current Chairman—and, I’m quite confident, the future Chair—I’m very comfortable with those explanations.	92	9
201401	178	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	I do welcome the prospect of further discussion of the entire statement.	92	9
201401	179	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Some of you may recall that my original concerns a couple of years ago were focused on the absence of what I thought to be an explicit enough statement of our having a symmetrical loss function with respect to the two policy aims of the dual mandate set forth in the Federal Reserve Act, which is, after all, the source of this Committee’s powers.	92	9
201401	180	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	I continue to have those concerns, although, more recently, I join Eric in having some concerns about the way in which the inflation number is actually understood—whether it is understood as a target, properly stated, or as more of a ceiling.	92	9
201401	181	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	I dare say that some members of the Committee would be distressed with a forecast that inflation would be 2.6 percent, 2.4 percent, and 2.3 percent, respectively, over the next several years.	92	9
201401	182	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	But, as we sit here today, we have a forecast of inflation at 1.4 percent, 1.6 percent, and 1.7 percent, respectively, over the next three years—that is to say, deviating on the downside by exactly the amounts hypothesized as upside deviations a moment ago.	92	9
201401	183	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	I want to be clear:  I pose this hypothetical not to argue for a specific policy response, but just to draw attention to what I think is at least a latent issue with respect to the stated goal of 2 percent inflation in the current statement.	92	9
201401	184	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	So when it comes time for Committee deliberation on this, I would be very interested in hearing an elaboration of not only the points that have already been made by some of our colleagues but also, I hope, the points that will be made by others of you.	92	9

201401	224	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Item 8, “Financial Developments and Open Market Operations.”	61	6
201401	225	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Let me turn to Simon Potter.	61	6
201401	226	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Thank you, Mr Chairman.	61	6
201401	227	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Markets responded positively to the Committee’s decision at the December meeting to reduce the pace of asset purchases, with a rise in equity prices and stable longer-term interest rates.	61	6
201401	228	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Shorter-term interest rates rose the day after the meeting, however, as some investors reportedly viewed the Committee’s qualitative modification to its forward rate guidance as less forceful than other options it was thought to be considering.	61	6
201401	229	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Since then, markets have fluctuated in response to economic data and, most recently, an increase in concerns regarding financial and economic stability in some emerging market economies.	61	6
201401	230	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	These concerns pushed U.S equity prices and interest rates significantly lower last week.	61	6
201401	231	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the first column of the top-left panel of your initial exhibit, using a slightly longer event window than usual to compensate for the extended horizon over which market participants reportedly digested the information provided by the Committee, short-dated rates increased and risk assets rallied following the December meeting.	61	6
201401	232	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the second column, on net over the intermeeting period, the implied rate on the December 2016 Eurodollar futures contract increased, while the 10-year nominal Treasury yield and 30-year primary mortgage rate declined.	61	6
201401	233	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Option- adjusted spreads on high-yield corporate credit narrowed, and the S&P 500 and DXY dollar indexes were little changed.	61	6
201401	234	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the top-right panel, shorter-term US interest rates and domestic equity prices fluctuated around the levels reached after the digestion of the FOMC decision, until conflicting labor market data introduced turbulence into short rates.	61	6
201401	235	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Toward the end of last week, increased concerns about growth and financial stability in China and other emerging market economies pushed US interest rates and risk asset prices significantly lower.	61	6
201401	236	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Market participants have highlighted several factors in explaining this pattern of movements in short-dated rates and equity prices.	61	6
201401	237	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	First, the FOMC’s modification to its forward rate guidance at the December meeting was perceived as less forceful than other options that the Committee was thought to be considering alongside a reduction in asset purchases, prompting a rise in short-dated rates the day after the meeting.	61	6
201401	238	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Second, fixed-income markets were relatively stable in the immediate wake of the announcement of the reduction in the pace of asset purchases; the passage of this risk event without the initial adverse market impact that some had expected supported risk asset prices.	61	6
201401	239	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Third, investors’ confidence in the economic outlook improved over the early part of the period, in part because of the Committee’s policy action and communications at the December meeting, as well as some better-than-expected economic data.	61	6
201401	240	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The dark-blue bars in the middle-left panel show the net changes in nominal one- year forward Treasury rates over the intermeeting period.	61	6
201401	241	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	One-year rates two to three years forward increased, while one-year rates six to nine years forward declined notably.	61	6
201401	242	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	In addition to reflecting a small upward shift in the expected target rate path, the rise in short-dated forwards likely reflects some increase in uncertainty regarding the target rate path, especially as the unemployment rate approaches the 6½ percent threshold.	61	6
201401	243	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Indeed, matching the moves in short-dated forward rates, three-month implied volatility on shorter-dated tenors increased over the period, as shown by the light-blue bars.	61	6
201401	244	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The decline in longer-dated forward rates and implied volatility at those long horizons may reflect some reduction in term premiums due to the less-adverse-than- expected impact of the “taper” announcement, as well as some reduction in uncertainty about the future path of the Federal Reserve’s asset purchases.	61	6
201401	245	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Demand for Treasury securities driven by developments in emerging markets last week also appears to have contributed to the fall in longer-dated forward rates.	61	6
201401	246	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As shown to the middle right, the market-implied target rate path now lies very close to the path implied by the median of year-end projections from the December SEP.	61	6
201401	247	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	This stands in contrast to mid-November, when the market-implied path had fallen notably below the path implied by SEP projections.	61	6
201401	248	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Results from the Desk’s latest Survey of Primary Dealers indicate a modest shift up in the expected target rate path since the survey conducted ahead of the December meeting, and dealers lowered their point estimates of the unemployment rate at the time of liftoff.	61	6
201401	249	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	This shift likely reflects the large decline in the unemployment rate relative to the disappointing improvement in other labor market indicators in the December employment report as well as the enhancement to forward guidance in the December FOMC statement.	61	6
201401	250	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As noted previously, some market participants expected a more explicit strengthening of forward guidance with the decision to reduce the pace of purchases.	61	6
201401	251	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	While short-dated rates decreased rather than increased following the unexpected fall in the unemployment rate to within 20 basis points of the threshold, market participants are increasingly turning their attention to how forward guidance will evolve after the threshold is reached.	61	6
201401	252	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Many appear to be relying on their expectation for a steady reduction in purchase pace of $10 billion at each meeting—in conjunction with the Committee’s view that a highly accommodative stance of monetary policy will remain appropriate for a considerable time after the asset purchase program ends—as a form of near-term rate guidance.	61	6
201401	253	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Bill English will discuss some of the changes in forward guidance that dealers think are likely.	61	6
201401	254	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	One measure of the dispersion of market views on the relationship between the unemployment rate and liftoff is given in the bottom-left panel.	61	6
201401	255	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Dealers’ current probability assessments for the unemployment rate at liftoff are shown in blue, alongside results from the Desk’s first pilot survey of buy-side market participants, shown in red.	61	6
201401	256	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The pilot survey aims to understand the expectations of active investment decisionmakers.	61	6
201401	257	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	While the average of beliefs is remarkably similar across the two sets of respondents, both sets of respondents show considerable dispersion of beliefs, with some buy-side respondents putting high odds on liftoff at unemployment rates close to the current level.	61	6
201401	258	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	More generally, the dealers and buy-side market participants have broadly similar expectations for asset purchases, SOMA holdings, the path of the target rate, and changes to the forward rate guidance.	61	6
201401	259	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	We will continue to analyze the surveys over time to understand how the expectations of the dealer economists and investment managers compare.	61	6
201401	260	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the bottom-right panel, the five-year TIPS-based measure of inflation compensation increased 10 basis points over the period, while the five-year measure five years forward declined 10 basis points.	61	6
201401	261	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Both measures remained within the relatively tight range that prevailed over recent months and seemed little affected by monetary policy developments.	61	6
201401	262	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Many market participants anticipate that an “attack on the forward guidance” is most likely to occur if measures of inflation expectations start to move up out of recent ranges.	61	6
201401	263	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As indicated in your next exhibit, the policy outlook in the United Kingdom and the euro area was also in focus over the intermeeting period.	61	6
201401	264	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	In the United Kingdom, the unemployment rate has approached the Bank of England’s 7 percent threshold faster than many had expected, pressuring short-term rates higher.	61	6
201401	265	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	This has increased uncertainty regarding how the BOE will adjust its forward guidance after the threshold is reached and regarding the pace of policy normalization thereafter.	61	6
201401	266	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	This uncertainty is reflected in higher levels of short-dated interest rates and swaption- implied volatility, the latter of which is shown for the United Kingdom and the United States in the top-left panel.	61	6
201401	267	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Despite their recent rise, both remain below the levels reached during mid-2013.	61	6
201401	268	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	By contrast, short-dated euro-area swaption-implied volatility and EURIBOR rates were little changed to modestly lower over the intermeeting period.	61	6
201401	269	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	This relative stability reflects continued expectations that inflation will run at below-target levels in the euro area for some time, as well as the ECB’s reiteration of its forward rate guidance.	61	6
201401	270	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The ECB’s current policy stance, along with the backstop provided by the OMT, has been an important factor contributing to the continued narrowing in euro-area peripheral sovereign debt spreads.	61	6
201401	271	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the top-right panel, these spreads are now at their tightest levels since at least August 2011.	61	6
201401	272	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Another factor contributing to the narrowing over recent months is greater confidence in the region’s growth outlook.	61	6
201401	273	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Amid ongoing risks in emerging markets, some foreign investors are shifting capital from emerging markets to the periphery.	61	6
201401	274	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Spread tightening since the start of 2014 also appears related to the passage of the reference date for the ECB’s asset quality review, as some peripheral banks reportedly repurchased domestic sovereign debt that they had previously shed for window-dressing purposes.	61	6
201401	275	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Together, these factors have improved access to capital markets for peripheral sovereign issuers, evidenced by strong demand and lower rates at recent auctions and syndications.	61	6
201401	276	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The improvement in the periphery has also extended to euro-area risk assets, as shown in the middle-left panel.	61	6
201401	277	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Euro-area equities have increased about 3 percent over the intermeeting period.	61	6
201401	278	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Some of the sharpest increases have been in bank shares, with the Euro STOXX bank index up almost 10 percent.	61	6
201401	279	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Your middle-right panel shows that emerging market currencies depreciated and local bond yields increased following the December FOMC decision.	61	6
201401	280	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Initially, these moves were relatively modest and orderly in most countries.	61	6
201401	281	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Since the beginning of the year, however, emerging market currencies and local bond prices have resumed their earlier fast declines.	61	6
201401	282	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Sentiment toward emerging market assets continues to be clouded by a number of interrelated concerns, including structural headwinds to emerging market growth; ongoing risks related to the cost and availability of external financing as market participants focus on the prospects for normalization of US monetary policy; questions about how China will navigate growth and financial- stability risks; and the potential for political and social unrest, underscored by recent developments in Turkey and Thailand.	61	6
201401	283	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	These concerns intensified last week, following a weaker-than-expected Chinese PMI reading, a selloff of the Turkish lira, and Argentina’s devaluation.	61	6
201401	284	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Steve will discuss the implications of these developments further in his briefing.	61	6
201401	285	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Market participants’ views on China are highly dispersed, and most admit to having a limited understanding of Chinese economic and financial developments.	61	6
201401	286	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	For example, there are differing interpretations of spikes in Chinese interbank funding rates, the most recent of which occurred in December and mid-January, as shown in the bottom-left panel.	61	6
201401	287	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Some have suggested that these spikes are due to the inability of Chinese authorities to effectively manage interbank funding markets.	61	6
201401	288	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Indeed, the PBOC recently expanded its short-term liquidity facility to smaller banks that typically demand interbank liquidity at penalty rates; this may better allow for management of episodic strains.	61	6
201401	289	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	However, others suspect that liquidity strains are intended as a signal for smaller banks to rein in rapid off-balance-sheet credit expansion.	61	6
201401	290	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Although Chinese aggregate credit growth—or total financing—has decelerated since the summer, its expansion has remained robust and may be higher than the authorities’ presumed comfort level.	61	6
201401	291	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The opaqueness of China, particularly with respect to its evolving financial structure, has caused some global investors to become concerned with financial- stability risks that could be posed by developments on the mainland.	61	6
201401	292	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Many have pointed to the recent growth of Chinese “shadow banking” and the lack of a uniform strategy among financial regulators to stem its expansion as one such risk.	61	6
201401	293	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	For example, market participants were recently focused on how Chinese financial regulators might treat future defaults on wealth-management or trust products and the implications of that for the Chinese banking sector.	61	6
201401	294	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Although there are limited windows to understanding Chinese risk appetite and financial stresses, some have pointed to the Shanghai Composite Index, an admittedly flawed indicator shown in the bottom-right panel, as the best metric for gaining some insight.	61	6
201401	295	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	This index declined significantly over the intermeeting period.	61	6
201401	296	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Turning to recent Desk operations, Treasury and agency MBS purchases since the December meeting have proceeded smoothly, with no issues arising from reduced market liquidity over year-end.	61	6
201401	297	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Further, we are not seeing signs of any notable impact directly related to the reduction in the pace of purchases.	61	6
201401	298	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Spreads on agency MBS narrowed a bit during the intermeeting period despite the shift lower in the expected total stock of asset purchases.	61	6
201401	299	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	According to the Survey of Primary Dealers, expectations for the overall amount of Federal Reserve asset purchases have declined about $90 billion since December, driven largely by the Committee’s decision to reduce the pace of purchases and the Chairman’s comments at the postmeeting press conference.	61	6
201401	300	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the solid line in the top panel of your third exhibit, the pace of purchases is expected to gradually decline over coming months.	61	6
201401	301	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The median expectation is that the Committee will reduce the pace of purchases in $10 billion increments at each meeting—split equally between Treasuries and MBS—until ending the program following the October meeting.	61	6
201401	302	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Thereafter, the portfolio is projected to remain steady for some time before beginning to shrink through MBS paydowns starting in mid- 2015, ahead of the expected timing of liftoff.	61	6
201401	303	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Treasury holdings are not expected to decline meaningfully until 2016.	61	6
201401	304	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The Treasury will auction its first floating-rate note tomorrow; the Desk’s work to build operational capacity in these securities is ongoing.	61	6
201401	305	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Separately, we have recently completed the initial testing of small-value MBS operations on our FedTrade platform, including the first outright sale, and intend to gradually incorporate FedTrade into our ongoing MBS operations in the coming months.	61	6
201401	306	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	A final note related to purchases:  If the Committee were to decide to reduce the pace of purchases at this meeting, the Desk would release a statement at the same time as the FOMC statement indicating that the new pace for Treasury securities and MBS will take effect at the start of February, similar to the statement that was released after the December meeting.	61	6
201401	307	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	I will now turn to recent developments in money markets and the daily overnight RRP operations and discuss the staff’s recommendation for continuing the exercise.	61	6
201401	308	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the middle-left panel, the number of participants and total allotment in the overnight RRP operations increased in late December.	61	6
201401	309	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Usage was particularly heavy on year-end, when take-up totaled $198 billion across 102 counterparties.	61	6
201401	310	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Since then, take-up has averaged around $66 billion, considerably above levels that prevailed prior to the December meeting.	61	6
201401	311	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The increase in usage is due in large part to continued low overnight secured rates relative to the fixed rate offered in the operations.	61	6
201401	312	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The middle-right panel shows that usage in the exercise remains quite sensitive to the spread between the rate offered on overnight RRPs and market rates for Treasury GC repo.	61	6
201401	313	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	It also has some sensitivity to nonprice factors, driven in particular by shifts in balance sheet management behavior around financial statement dates.	61	6
201401	314	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The higher usage is also attributable to the increase in the per-counterparty bid limit from $1 billion to $3 billion that was implemented on December 23.	61	6
201401	315	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	This has obviously allowed for increased take-up in operations.	61	6
201401	316	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As shown in the bottom-left panel, on December 31, 35 counterparties took advantage of the larger cap by submitting maximum bids—representing 53 percent of the total amount awarded— and 47 other counterparties submitted bids for $1 billion or more.	61	6
201401	317	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Outside of year- end, the number of counterparties bidding for the maximum amount is typically relatively small, though many counterparties are taking up more than the prior cap of $1 billion.	61	6
201401	318	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The increase in usage since the December meeting has been principally driven by government-only and prime money market mutual funds, which are shown in the dark-blue and light-blue bars in the bottom-right panel.	61	6
201401	319	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	On year-end, the GSEs and primary dealers also increased their participation, with primary dealer usage remaining surprisingly high in January.	61	6
201401	320	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As I mentioned earlier, usage in the operations is sensitive to the spread between the rate offered on overnight RRPs and comparable overnight secured rates.	61	6
201401	321	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The path of these rates is shown in the top-left panel of your final exhibit; it can be seen here that the tightening in this spread was driven partly by a drop in market rates, with some of this drop occurring when the rate on the facility was lowered to 3 basis points.	61	6
201401	322	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The decline in rates around year-end was attributed in part to decreased primary dealer repo activity, which has been typical over recent year-ends, as well as lower borrowing in dollar funding markets by foreign banks.	61	6
201401	323	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Another important factor contributing to lower overnight secured rates over recent weeks has been declines in bill supply, shown in the upper-right panel.	61	6
201401	324	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Declines in bill supply are largely due to seasonal factors and the Treasury managing down its cash position in advance of the statutory debt limit, which will become binding on February 7.	61	6
201401	325	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	After this date, the Treasury is expected to utilize extraordinary measures to fund the government.	61	6
201401	326	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Secretary Lew publicly estimated that these measures will be exhausted in late February, though others estimate that this will occur in mid- March.	61	6
201401	327	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Correspondingly, there is a slight kink in the Treasury bill curve around these dates, reflecting some investor reluctance to hold securities with potentially delayed payments; the effects of this kink can be seen in the one-month Treasury bill rate shown in light blue in the top-left panel.	61	6
201401	328	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Market participants have reported that money market rates would have been much lower and likely negative around year-end if not for our operations.	61	6
201401	329	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	They continue to note their expectations that overnight RRP operations could provide a firm floor for money market rates if implemented in full scale.	61	6
201401	330	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	However, several features of the current exercise may limit the extent to which we see this in practice:  the short time frame over which the exercise is authorized, which reportedly creates hesitancy on the part of some of our counterparties to move away from their existing relationships; limited bargaining power on the part of those with access to the facility; and some operational frictions, including around the timing of the operations.	61	6
201401	331	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Finally, many market participants also note that it remains unclear whether the current number and mix of counterparties are sufficient.	61	6
201401	332	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	As described in a memo circulated to the Committee in advance of the meeting, the staff’s assessment is that there would be benefits to extending the exercise beyond the end of January, with enhancements to the terms that may help mitigate some of the issues I just noted.	61	6
201401	333	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	This would give us further insight into how operations might influence money market rates and may give the Committee greater confidence in the use of overnight RRPs in normalizing policy.	61	6
201401	334	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	In particular, as outlined in the middle- left panel, the staff seeks to better understand the extent to which overnight RRPs are able to establish a floor on money market rates, to evaluate the impact of adding counterparties on effectiveness, and to assess the feasibility and impact of operating later in the day or possibly twice in one day.	61	6
201401	335	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Learning more soon about the potential effectiveness of a facility allows time for further development of or adjustments to the operations and associated testing, as well as for enhancements of other tools, if needed.	61	6
201401	336	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	To this end, as shown in the middle-right panel, the staff recommends extending the overnight RRP exercise for one year; raising the allotment cap in a series of steps; and, assuming no adverse developments, moving gradually to full allotment over the coming months.	61	6
201401	337	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	The staff also recommends continuing to operate the overnight RRP tests within a band of 0 to 5 basis points.	61	6
201401	338	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	If the Committee agrees with these recommendations, the staff would release a Desk statement at the same time as the FOMC statement tomorrow afternoon, as described in appendix 2 of the memo that we sent to you.	61	6
201401	339	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Any changes in terms would require approval of the Chair, and, as noted in the memo, the Committee would be consulted before approval of a recommendation to go to full allotment.	61	6
201401	340	2014-01-29	meeting	POTTER	Thank you, Mr Chairman.	61	6

201401	345	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Just to clarify, the proposal is as follows:  First, extend this exercise for one year in order to allow more continuity with counterparties so that they can count on having a relationship that extends more than meeting to meeting.	62	6

201401	349	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Let me say that, in parallel to that—and again, I have conferred with Governor Yellen— staff work is under way that will look at the intermediate operating procedures that we might consider, the long-run operating procedures that we might consider, the effects of these types of facilities on the funds rate and the federal funds market, and the possibility of switching to a different interest rate indicator for our monetary policy communication.	62	6

201401	671	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Let’s turn now to item 9, “Economic Situation,” and I’ll call on David Wilcox for the presentation.	11	1
201401	672	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Thank you, Mr Chairman.	11	1
201401	673	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	I’ll be referring to the single exhibit titled “Material for Forecast Summary.”	11	1
201401	674	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	For the most part, the economic recovery appears now to be on firmer ground than it did at the time of the December meeting.	11	1
201401	675	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	To be sure, even Garrison Keillor would concede that not all of the news that we received over the intermeeting period was above average, but much of it was.	11	1
201401	676	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	As you can see from the top-left panel of the “Forecast Summary” exhibit, the incoming spending data caused us to mark up our estimate of second-half real GDP growth by a little more than 1 percentage point, with consumption, business investment, and foreign trade all contributing.	11	1
201401	677	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	The upward revision to consumer spending was particularly encouraging and brings that category of spending into closer alignment with the predictions of some of the models that we follow.	11	1
201401	678	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	We still have the growth of real PCE stepping down in the current quarter relative to the torrid fourth-quarter pace, partly because we continue to assume that the Congress will not reverse the expiration of the Emergency Unemployment Compensation program that took place at the turn of the year.	11	1
201401	679	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	As in the previous Tealbook, we expect the expiration of this program to subtract about ½ percentage point from real consumption growth in the current quarter.	11	1
201401	680	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Today’s advance durables report was one of the flies in Garrison Keillor’s ointment, but, even folding in this news, we still have the level of equipment spending a little higher in the current quarter than we did six weeks ago.	11	1
201401	681	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Another fly in the ointment came from residential investment.	11	1
201401	682	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	While December’s single-family starts figure retraced only some of November’s jump, single-family permits—which we generally look to as providing a better gauge of activity in the sector—surprised us to the downside in December.	11	1
201401	683	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	So where do we stand in the broader recovery of housing activity?	11	1
201401	684	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	First, we think the most intense phase of adjustment to last year’s upward movement in mortgage interest rates is likely behind us.	11	1
201401	685	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	We take some encouragement in this regard from the flattening out of existing home sales and the brighter tone of recent readings on builder, Realtor, and homebuyer sentiment.	11	1
201401	686	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Second, while future increases in rates will exert further restraint, we continue to think that, in the medium term, the basic arithmetic of the situation is that activity has to move up from its current level, because otherwise we will be running an ongoing deficit relative to demographically driven demands.	11	1
201401	687	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	But the latest data raise important questions about how quickly homebuilding will resume that more normal pace, and those are the questions we’ll be focusing on quite intently in future rounds.	11	1
201401	688	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	In previous Tealbooks, we raised the possibility that the then-available estimates of real GDP were misleadingly weak, and that the faster growth of real GDI and the ongoing declines in the unemployment rate might have been providing the truer signals about the strength of the recovery.	11	1
201401	689	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	At the risk of tempting fate, it’s hard not to regard the brighter tone of the incoming data over the intermeeting period as giving that hypothesis greater credence.	11	1
201401	690	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Our projection for real GDP growth over the next few years is, nonetheless, at this point, little revised from December, as two opposing forces fought each other roughly to a draw: On the one hand, aggregate demand seems to have a little greater momentum than before.	11	1
201401	691	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	On the other hand, some of the other factors that we condition our forecast on have become a little less supportive of growth.	11	1
201401	692	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Chief among these is the trajectory for the foreign exchange value of the dollar, which we’ve adjusted upward relative to our assumption in the December Tealbook.	11	1
201401	693	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	In addition, as we described in the Tealbook, we have the funds rate coming up a little more quickly than we did in December, and the steeper increase in the funds rate drives a slightly faster rise in longer-term rates.	11	1
201401	694	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Turning to the labor market, the December employment report was a mixed bag.	11	1
201401	695	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	On the establishment side, total nonfarm payroll employment rose only 74,000 in December.	11	1
201401	696	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Even accounting for the likely effect of last month’s bad weather—which we estimate to be on the order of about 20,000 jobs—December’s gain was well below our forecast.	11	1
201401	697	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	For the most part, we looked through the disappointment in payroll employment, as you can see in the top-right panel, and left our jobs forecast over the medium term essentially unrevised relative to December.	11	1
201401	698	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	On the household side, the puzzles were, if anything, even bigger.	11	1
201401	699	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	The unemployment rate—shown in the middle-left panel—declined 0.3 percentage point last month, to 6.7 percent, whereas we had been projecting no change.	11	1
201401	700	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	At the same time, the labor force participation rate—not shown—declined 0.2 percentage point; again, we had been expecting no change.	11	1
201401	701	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	As you know, and as Charles Fleischman reiterated in his briefing yesterday, we’ve been too pessimistic about the unemployment rate for a couple of years now, despite a pretty good track record with respect to the growth of real GDP.	11	1
201401	702	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	To make a very, very, very long story considerably shorter, we trimmed our assumption for the growth of potential GDP, both during the past few years and, to a lesser extent, going forward.	11	1
201401	703	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Mechanically, this adjustment allows us to better explain the decline in the unemployment rate during the past couple of years, particularly given that we wanted to scale back the role of the panic-and-normalization story that had, until now, featured more prominently in our narrative.	11	1
201401	704	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	As a result of the changes we made to the supply side—and the surprises to actual output and the unemployment rate that we’ve seen since the December Tealbook— we now have an output gap at the end of last year that’s ½ percentage point narrower than in our previous projection and an unemployment gap that is ¼ percentage point narrower.	11	1
201401	705	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Moreover, we project that the unemployment rate will cross your 6½ percent threshold around the middle of this year and will lie just below our assumed 5¼ percent natural rate at the end of 2016.	11	1
201401	706	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	We also took another look during the intermeeting period at the behavior of the labor force participation rate in recent years.	11	1
201401	707	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	In a memo that you received last week, Tomaz Cajner and Bruce Fallick conclude that about half of the reduction in the participation rate since 2007 is due to demographic factors—specifically, the aging of the population.	11	1
201401	708	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	As the middle-right panel shows, participation rates for men and women drop off sharply around age 65; hence, the increasing share of those aged 65 or above in the overall population has yielded a secular decline in the aggregate participation rate.	11	1
201401	709	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	This trend has been exacerbated by changes in group-specific participation rates—such as reductions in participation among younger and prime-age individuals—an important share of which also appears to be structural rather than cyclical in nature.	11	1
201401	710	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	The fact that the decline in aggregate participation late last year coincided with a surprising decline in the unemployment rate certainly raises the question as to whether the two developments might be causally linked.	11	1
201401	711	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Until now, we’ve been skeptical of such a linkage, based on previous historical experience.	11	1
201401	712	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	But in light of the magnitude of the surprises in the two variables, this round our baseline projection incorporates the possibility that unusual weakness in participation might help explain the surprising decline in the unemployment rate.	11	1
201401	713	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	This is a linkage that we’ll obviously be scrutinizing going forward.	11	1
201401	714	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Finally, with respect to inflation:  The recent data have come in about as we expected; as you can see from the bottom-left panel, we’ve edged up our core inflation forecast over the medium term to reflect the slightly narrower margin of slack in this projection.	11	1
201401	715	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	The panel on the bottom right decomposes the contour of our core PCE projection in terms of its fundamental determinants.	11	1
201401	716	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	A similar exercise was undertaken for the December Tealbook forecast in the recent memo on the staff inflation outlook by Alan Detmeister, Jean-Philippe Laforte, and Jeremy Rudd.	11	1
201401	717	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	We expect core inflation to step up this year, reflecting an acceleration in import prices and a reduced influence of some other factors that kept core inflation low in 2013 but that we think will prove largely transitory.	11	1
201401	718	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Thereafter, core inflation edges up gradually as inflation expectations remain anchored near the Committee’s target, and the amount of economic slack diminishes.	11	1
201401	719	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	The projected contour of headline PCE inflation—not shown—is broadly similar, though projected declines in consumer energy prices leave headline inflation running just below core.	11	1
201401	720	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Steve Kamin will now continue our remarks.	11	1
201401	721	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	I’ll be referring to the handout titled “Material for the Foreign Economic Outlook.”	11	1
201401	722	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	As indicated in panel 1, in the top-left corner, after several years of weak and choppy performance, the economies of our trading partners appear set for solid, sustained economic growth.	11	1
201401	723	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	The global financial turbulence that erupted after we put the Tealbook to bed last week could, in principle, snowball and derail this recovery, but we don’t consider that to be the most likely outcome.	11	1
201401	724	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	But before discussing this risk, I’ll briefly review our forecast.	11	1
201401	725	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Starting with the advanced foreign economies, the solid black line in panel 1, their rebound actually started early last year as the euro-area recession ended, the UK economy emerged from its doldrums, and Japanese growth surged on the back of Abenomics-inspired fiscal and monetary expansion.	11	1
201401	726	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	We estimate that average growth in the AFEs remained solid last quarter and should edge up a little more over the next several years as the euro-area recovery picks up speed, more than offsetting a moderation in Japan’s growth to more sustainable rates.	11	1
201401	727	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	The emerging market economies (EMEs), the red line, have also rebounded from their earlier weakness, and we estimate that GDP growth notched up further in the fourth quarter.	11	1
201401	728	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	China’s growth slowed a touch, but to a still quite solid 8 percent, while activity in the other Asian EMEs appears to have accelerated as exports strengthened.	11	1
201401	729	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	For Latin America, we estimate that the Mexican economy grew at a 3½ percent pace for the second quarter in a row, while Brazilian GDP picked up after contracting in the third quarter.	11	1
201401	730	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	We are looking for just a bit higher growth for the EMEs in the remainder of the forecast period, driven importantly by stronger demand from the advanced economies, including the United States.	11	1
201401	731	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	With economic data from abroad generally coming in as we’d expected, our forecast is essentially the same as the one we wrote down in December, as you can almost see by looking at the dotted lines in panel 1.	11	1
201401	732	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	In the Tealbook, we identified two reasons why, despite the fact that we did not alter our projection, our confidence in the global recovery had strengthened.	11	1
201401	733	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	First, we noted that, in peripheral Europe, financial stresses had continued to ease and output is bottoming out, further reducing the likelihood of a resurgence of the euro-area crisis.	11	1
201401	734	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	We stand by that call.	11	1
201401	735	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Second, we noted that your decision to taper asset purchases at the December meeting had been largely taken in stride by emerging financial markets, and this boded well for future stability in the region.	11	1
201401	736	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Here, the turbulence in global markets that erupted late last week has given us pause.	11	1
201401	737	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	As Simon noted, market commentary attributed the global selloff in risky assets late last week to a number of disparate events—a low PMI reading for China, the selloff of the Turkish lira, and Argentina’s devaluation—that may signal a sharp decline in emerging market economic growth.	11	1
201401	738	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	We are skeptical that these developments support such a sharp downgrade in either the emerging market or the global outlook.	11	1
201401	739	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	In particular, the decline in the flash PMI for Chinese manufacturing in January to 49.6, which was cited in many media reports, does not signal outright contraction in output.	11	1
201401	740	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Rather, it is entirely consistent with our forecast of only a gradual moderation of Chinese growth in the current and next few quarters.	11	1
201401	741	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	All of that said, however, even if this bout of financial turbulence is due more to an upswing in risk aversion than to fundamental factors, were it to persist and intensify, it could still damp the global economic recovery and thus bears watching closely.	11	1
201401	742	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Beyond the risks of financial flare-ups in emerging markets, we have also been focused on the possibility of deflation in the advanced foreign economies.	11	1
201401	743	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Panel 2 shows that inflation rates in some of the major AFEs have moved down along with US rates in the past couple of years.	11	1
201401	744	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	To shed more light on this development, panel 3 reproduces from a box in the Tealbook a decomposition of movements in the average inflation rate of the advanced foreign economies excluding Japan.	11	1
201401	745	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	The decomposition is based on Phillips curve regressions that link quarterly headline inflation rates (the solid line) to survey measures of inflation expectations, the output gap, supply factors such as oil and import prices, and consumption taxes.	11	1
201401	746	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Focusing on inflation between 2011 and 2013, much of the decline can be attributed to a widening output gap (the red bars) and, more important, the waning of earlier upward pressures from supply factors (the green bars) and tax hikes (the pink bars).	11	1
201401	747	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Going forward, declining oil prices and moderate currency appreciation should exert only modest further downward pressures.	11	1
201401	748	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	As the recovery in these economies progresses, reductions in resource slack should push inflation back up toward target levels, as shown in panel 2.	11	1
201401	749	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	However, this outlook depends critically on inflation expectations continuing to remain well anchored.	11	1
201401	750	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	An alternative simulation in the Tealbook shows that if, instead, inflation expectations start to drift down, real interest rates will rise, AFE economic activity will weaken, and thus US growth will be dragged down as well.	11	1
201401	751	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Foreign central banks are clearly focused on these risks.	11	1
201401	752	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	In November, the ECB cut rates when 12-month inflation unexpectedly fell to 0.7 percent, and, more recently, Bank of Canada Governor Stephen Poloz noted that downside risks to inflation were a central concern, suggesting that the Bank of Canada might keep interest rates low for longer than previously anticipated.	11	1
201401	753	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Conversely, in the United Kingdom, where inflation has fallen close to the 2 percent target, the Bank of England faces a different problem, and one closely related to our own policy challenge.	11	1
201401	754	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	In August of last year, as marked by the vertical line in panel 4, the Bank’s Monetary Policy Committee announced it would not tighten policy at least until unemployment fell below 7 percent, a threshold it anticipated reaching in 2016.	11	1
201401	755	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	In the event, as indicated by the red line, the UK unemployment rate plummeted, and the threshold may already have been crossed.	11	1
201401	756	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Thus, the Bank of England is confronting the questions of whether falling unemployment rates reflect an actual narrowing in resource slack and how it should adjust its forward guidance in response.	11	1
201401	757	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Unlike in the United States, the decline in the UK unemployment rate has not been accompanied by falling labor force participation.	11	1
201401	758	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	As shown in panel 5, labor force participation has, in fact, risen slightly in recent years.	11	1
201401	759	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Instead, turning to panel 6, over the past couple of quarters, unexpectedly rapid employment growth, which has been associated with surprisingly weak productivity, has been the main driver of falling unemployment in the United Kingdom.	11	1
201401	760	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	Although rapidly rising employment suggests narrowing output gaps, Bank of England staff members point to a number of reasons why they believe that sufficient slack remains to obviate the need to raise interest rates in the near future: Inflation has been declining and wage growth has been weak; the unemployment rate remains above their estimates of the natural rate; average weekly hours remain depressed; and they continue to hope that, just as earlier declines in GDP were associated with falls in productivity, resurgent output growth will lead to a recovery of productivity.	11	1
201401	761	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	They plan to elaborate on these issues in their February Inflation Report.	11	1
201401	762	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	We do not know how the Bank of England’s policymakers will adjust their forward guidance, but our sense is that they are less likely to lower their unemployment rate threshold than to emphasize, as we have done, that they will look at a range of indicators and likely hold the policy rate low until well after the threshold is crossed.	11	1
201401	763	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	This concludes my prepared remarks.	11	1
201401	764	2014-01-29	meeting	KAMIN	David and I will be happy to answer your questions.	11	1

201401	1031	2014-01-29	meeting	WASCHER	We’re going to get the 2013 revision next month—we already know that it’s a downward revision if you adjust for some classification changes.	12	1
201401	1032	2014-01-29	meeting	WASCHER	But the revisions in both 2011 and 2012 went the other way; the payroll survey was revised up.	12	1
201401	1033	2014-01-29	meeting	WASCHER	And I think the general pattern is that, early in recoveries, you tend to see some upward revisions.	12	1
201401	1034	2014-01-29	meeting	WASCHER	That’s probably due to increases in new business formation that aren’t picked up initially in the payroll survey.	12	1
201401	1035	2014-01-29	meeting	WASCHER	Beyond that, there tend to be downward revisions during recessions and weak periods.	12	1
201401	1036	2014-01-29	meeting	WASCHER	So, from 2007 to 2010, there were downward revisions, and then in 2011 and 2012, there were upward revisions to the level of employment.	12	1
201401	1037	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	I have one other observation.	12	1
201401	1038	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	I don’t want to make too big a deal of this, because I’ll get in over my head very quickly, but, in terms of the inflation issue, one of the things that many of the DSGE models have—and I think the New York model has this in it in some detail—is that the inflation process in those models depends a lot on the nature of the shocks.	12	1
201401	1039	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	In the financial sectors that they have, you can have these shocks to the return on capital or the marginal efficiency of capital.	12	1
201401	1040	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	And those things can actually work through the system in ways that can lead, at least in those models, to lower inflation than you might otherwise have anticipated.	12	1
201401	1041	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	It’s the nature of the financial sector and the restrictions in the financial sector that have given rise to some of that.	12	1
201401	1042	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	So I was wondering whether or not more work has been done in thinking about those types of models—as opposed to the classic, more traditional models that you report to us on—in terms of the effects that different types of shocks have on generating the lower inflation that we’ve seen.	12	1
201401	1043	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	I don’t know—is Egon Zakrajšek here?	12	1
201401	1044	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	If he’s not, then I’ll attempt to characterize some recent research that he’s done.	12	1
201401	1045	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	You’ll probably do better at characterizing that than I did in attempting to characterize my position here in the first place.	12	1
201401	1046	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	I don’t know—Jeremy or Michael Kiley, are you familiar with this, before I launch into an attempt here?	12	1
201401	1047	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Broadly speaking, what Egon and his coauthors are trying to explain is, why didn’t we have more disinflation?	12	1
201401	1048	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	It’s a fascinating paper.	12	1
201401	1049	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	They are working with microeconomic data.	12	1
201401	1050	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	They split firms into two buckets:  One contains firms whose balance sheets were very adversely affected through the financial crisis, and the other bucket, the complement.	12	1
201401	1051	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	What they find, I believe, is that the firms whose balance sheets were severely adversely affected through the financial crisis are much more resistant to allowing price reductions, whereas firms whose balance sheets were not particularly affected by the financial crisis behave in a more historically normal manner.	12	1
201401	1052	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	And it’s that mechanism that provides an alternative explanation, by the way, to the anchored inflation expectations that they put forward as a possibility.	12	1
201401	1053	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	That’s not exactly what I was asking.	12	1
201401	1054	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	But we can do this offline.	12	1
201401	1055	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	My question is whether some of these models with financial frictions of various kinds get movements in inflation rates that are not as closely tied to monetary policy as we normally think.	12	1
201401	1056	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	That was the gist of what I think some of these models are trying to tell us, but we can talk about this later.	12	1
201401	1057	2014-01-29	meeting	WILCOX	Michael, do you want to offer any comments about that?	12	1
201401	1058	2014-01-29	meeting	KILEY	It’s certainly the case that the staff did a lot of work on a variety of DSGE models with different types of financial frictions and shocks and on how the different shocks are transmitted through those mechanisms.	12	1
201401	1059	2014-01-29	meeting	KILEY	You’re definitely right that those mechanisms and shocks can have effects on activity and inflation that differ from those of other demand shocks.	12	1
201401	1060	2014-01-29	meeting	KILEY	So it’s the case that financial frictions can alter the way in which shocks are transmitted both to the real economy and to inflation.	12	1
201401	1061	2014-01-29	meeting	KILEY	But I think it’s fair to say that there’s no common result across those models, because there are lots of financial frictions now.	12	1
201401	1062	2014-01-29	meeting	KILEY	And so I don’t think it would be fair to say that it’s a general feature that shocks, other than monetary shocks, become much more important in explaining inflation in some different way.	12	1
201401	1063	2014-01-29	meeting	KILEY	It’s a much more open area.	12	1
201401	1064	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	Very model dependent.	12	1
201401	1065	2014-01-29	meeting	KILEY	Yes.	12	1

201401	1088	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	This may work out just fine within six months’ time, but our understanding of these factors and the inflation equation itself is tenuous at best, and the risk of prolonged low inflation cannot be ruled out.	13	1
201401	1089	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	My business contacts contribute to my worries over this dilemma when they tell me that they have little pricing power and that cost pressures are low.	13	1
201401	1090	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	A key reason why cost pressures are low is that wage growth has been modest to meager.	13	1
201401	1091	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	We haven’t spent much time discussing wages.	13	1
201401	1092	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	That’s probably because, in recent decades, wages have not been a reliable leading indicator for inflation pressures.	13	1
201401	1093	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	Instead, wages have tended to lag inflation.	13	1
201401	1094	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	In any event, the low wage levels and wage growth we see today are, at a minimum, confirming evidence of the lack of inflationary pressures existing in the current economic environment.	13	1
201401	1095	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	These wage data suggest possibly more persistent influences on low inflation.	13	1
201401	1096	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	In order to get an outbreak of stronger and persistently higher inflation, we almost surely would have to have sympathetic increases in wages.	13	1
201401	1097	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	If such an outbreak were in the works, we’d expect to see workers demanding, and firms granting, notably higher wage increases, and this should be occurring broadly across occupational and industrial sectors.	13	1
201401	1098	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	Of course, none of this has taken place at the moment, and none of my business contacts think it is at all likely to happen for the foreseeable future.	13	1
201401	1099	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	Indeed, as the Board’s inflation forecasting memo discussed—and I should note, too, research by President Williams’s staff—because of downward nominal rigidity, it’s possible that wages have not yet fully adjusted to earlier weakness in labor markets.	13	1
201401	1100	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	That might mean we could see residual resistance to wage increases for some time, even as other labor market conditions further improve.	13	1
201401	1101	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	In sum, the wage-cost side of the ledger seems to suggest that inflation could remain low for some time.	13	1
201401	1102	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	This poses a substantial risk to achieving our inflation objective of 2 percent within a reasonable time frame.	13	1
201401	1103	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	At the moment, the primary reason for rising inflation in our projections is that we expect anchored long-term inflation expectations to exert an upward pull on actual inflation.	13	1
201401	1104	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	The inflation-forecasting models discussed in the Board memo place substantial emphasis on these long-term expectations, and much of my own staff’s analysis has a similar flavor.	13	1
201401	1105	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	I am not aware of a better inflation specification in spite of the commentary that I made earlier in asking questions.	13	1
201401	1106	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	Nevertheless, this is unsatisfying.	13	1
201401	1107	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	Most theoretically grounded specifications place a greater weight on short-term inflation expectations.	13	1
201401	1108	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	Of course, short-term expectations bounce around a lot more and pose difficult challenges for successful empirical implementation.	13	1
201401	1109	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	Most of the time, anchored long-term expectations work better empirically, and the discrepancy is smaller and more transitory than what we’ve had.	13	1
201401	1110	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	The Board’s modeling choice is completely understandable, but it feels unsatisfying and less structural than desired, and that’s especially the case now.	13	1

201401	1118	2014-01-29	meeting	EVANS	Until we actually see inflation move up smartly toward our 2 percent target, I think we are running a risk of misjudging the persistence of our low-inflation environment and relying too heavily on long-term expectations to save the day.	13	1

201401	1123	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Thank you, Mr Chairman.	13	1
201401	1124	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The memos have done a very nice job of clarifying staff members’ positions on the important but difficult issue of which labor market and price anomalies reflect transitory movements and which reflect trends.	13	1
201401	1125	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The very low payroll number last month is assumed to be an anomaly, suggesting that much stronger payroll employment growth is imminent.	13	1
201401	1126	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Similarly, part of the very low inflation numbers is an anomaly, suggesting that we will soon see PCE inflation moving toward our 2 percent target.	13	1
201401	1127	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	In contrast, the large declines in labor force participation are assumed to be permanent, reflecting primarily demographic and cohort trends.	13	1
201401	1128	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	While the parsing of these data patterns into transitory or trend categories seems reasonable, I would note with the benefit of hindsight that we have not always been able to distinguish well between what is transitory and what is a trend.	13	1
201401	1129	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	A simple example is the assumption that Internet investments during the dot-com phase reflected a change in trend productivity growth that could justify high stock prices rather than a transitory fluctuation, only to be revised down once the dot-com bubble burst.	13	1
201401	1130	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Thus, while assessments made by the staff are reasonable, our actions should reflect the inherent uncertainty around such judgments.	13	1
201401	1131	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The payroll employment number was a surprise.	13	1
201401	1132	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The 10-year Treasury rate moved down substantially after the announcement and has only fallen further with growing concerns around emerging markets.	13	1
201401	1133	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Financial markets seem to have taken to heart at least some of the surprise information, at least for now.	13	1
201401	1134	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The Tealbook makes clear that it is assumed that the low payroll employment is transitory, because it is incongruent with both the other labor market indicators and the stronger GDP growth we have seen recently.	13	1
201401	1135	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	However, the highly correlated movement of global stock prices surrounding emerging market concerns and the decline in the 10-year Treasury rate since our last meeting, despite strong GDP numbers and our strong GDP forecast, give me some pause about the forecast.	13	1
201401	1136	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The recent serially correlated misses on many of our PCE inflation forecasts are also assumed to be transitory.	13	1
201401	1137	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	However, the longer we miss significantly on our forecast, the less confidence we should have that inflation will be inexorably drawn higher by the gravitational force of our 2 percent target.	13	1
201401	1138	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The staff highlights that in Japan the long-term expectations for inflation moved little despite inflation being below zero for over a decade.	13	1
201401	1139	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The observation is very consistent with work my staff has done.	13	1
201401	1140	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	They show empirically that Japanese inflation data may have been more responsive to short-run inflation expectations than long-run expectations.	13	1
201401	1141	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	Similarly, the model that relies on well-anchored expectations fits the past 20 years of data reasonably well but is sensitive to the time period over which it is estimated.	13	1
201401	1142	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	If it turns out that, in very low inflation environments, short-run inflation expectations are more important than long-run expectations in describing the inflation process, we should be much less certain how quickly we will return to our 2 percent inflation target.	13	1
201401	1143	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The staff paper on labor force participation is good.	13	1
201401	1144	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The paper provides a nice statistical summary of both demographic and within-cohort trends.	13	1
201401	1145	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	While I agree with the demographic exposition, the within-cohort trends seem tenuous.	13	1
201401	1146	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	While there were sound economic reasons for the rise in labor force participation by women in the 1970s and 1980s, the economic rationale for a trend reduction in participation by prime-age male cohorts is less clear, and, thus, questions remain as to whether their lower participation rates will be reversed by a stronger labor market.	13	1
201401	1147	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	In summary, we should be cautious about our inferences regarding which data reflect transitory influences and which reflect trends.	13	1
201401	1148	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	The implications of this uncertainty for policy, which I will discuss tomorrow, suggest that a patient approach to removing accommodation is warranted.	13	1
