201401	1152	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Mr Chairman.	13	1
201401	1153	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I will spend most of my time today discussing my assessment of the long-run unemployment rate that is consistent with the dual mandate.	13	1
201401	1154	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I will make two points.	13	1
201401	1155	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	First, any assessment of this kind relies on gauging the inflationary pressures associated with any given unemployment rate.	13	1
201401	1156	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Second, over the past year or two I have lowered my assessment of the inflationary pressures associated with any given unemployment rate.	13	1
201401	1157	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As a result, my assessment of the long-run unemployment rate consistent with maximum employment has fallen 1 full percentage point, down to the 5¼ percent rate in Tealbook A.	13	1
201401	1158	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Let me start with the relationship between inflationary pressures and unemployment.	13	1
201401	1159	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We have agreed, and we just reaffirmed again today, that appropriate monetary policy should give rise to an inflation rate that averages 2 percent over the longer run with inflation expectations well anchored around that same rate.	13	1
201401	1160	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Of course, the mandate-consistent unemployment rate has to also be consistent with that kind of monetary policy.	13	1
201401	1161	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It follows that, over the longer run, the mandate-consistent unemployment rate is the unemployment rate that is consistent with 2 percent inflation with well-anchored inflation expectations.	13	1
201401	1162	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This observation has implications for how we go about measuring the long-run mandate- consistent unemployment rate.	13	1
201401	1163	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, there’s a lot of work being done in the System and elsewhere that attempts to do so by filtering out short-run fluctuations and various aspects of employment and unemployment data.	13	1
201401	1164	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	From a statistical point of view, this is hard work, and President Rosengren alluded to this in his remarks.	13	1
201401	1165	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Trend–cycle decompositions are challenging, and they’re especially challenging at the end of a sample.	13	1
201401	1166	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But the economic challenge is actually a deeper one.	13	1
201401	1167	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Conceptually, the mandate-consistent unemployment rate, as I just mentioned, is the unemployment rate that is consistent with 2 percent inflation and well-anchored inflation expectations.	13	1
201401	1168	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Estimating this unemployment rate necessarily requires information beyond quantity data alone.	13	1
201401	1169	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It requires information about the compensation pressures associated with any given unemployment rate.	13	1
201401	1170	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It is those compensation pressures that eventually translate into inflation in the prices of goods and services.	13	1
201401	1171	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Let me then move to my second point.	13	1
201401	1172	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Over the past year or two, I’ve lowered my assessment of the pressures on compensation associated with any given unemployment rate.	13	1
201401	1173	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Why have I done that?	13	1
201401	1174	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	There are three main reasons.	13	1
201401	1175	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	First, a falling unemployment rate over the past four years has been associated with little increase in wage and compensation pressures.	13	1
201401	1176	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Indeed, in the first three quarters of 2013, average hourly compensation in the nonfarm business sector was essentially flat.	13	1
201401	1177	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Second, for any given long-run unemployment rate, I’ve lowered my estimate of the fraction of prime-age people, those between the ages of 25 and 54, who will have jobs.	13	1
201401	1178	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’ve done so because, conditional on the falling unemployment rate, the employment-to-population ratio for 25- to 54-year-olds has risen surprisingly little.	13	1
201401	1179	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, marginally, I see a prime-age person without a job as putting more downward pressure on wages than a similar person with a job.	13	1
201401	1180	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s definitely hard to pin down magnitudes here, but the effect on the margin is to lower the inflationary impact of any given long-term unemployment rate because there are more prime-age people without jobs.	13	1
201401	1181	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Third, for any given unemployment rate, I’ve raised my estimate of those working part- time who would like to be able to work more hours.	13	1
201401	1182	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	So I’m tracking this through the difference between two broader measures of labor force underutilization, the ones labeled U-6 and U-5.	13	1
201401	1183	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The difference between U-6 and U-5 rose dramatically in the recession.	13	1
201401	1184	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Relative to the sharp decline in the unemployment rate, U-3, this difference has fallen little since late 2009.	13	1
201401	1185	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	This large number of people who are looking for extra hours are also likely to put more downward pressure on wages for any given measure of the unemployment rate.	13	1
201401	1186	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	At the national level, then, we’ve seen little in the way of wage pressures, we have a larger fraction of prime-age people who do not have jobs, and the difference between U-6 and U-5 has remained persistently high.	13	1
201401	1187	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, we go to the local conditions in the Ninth District.	13	1
201401	1188	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We have also heard suggestive evidence along the same lines.	13	1
201401	1189	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In Minnesota, the current unemployment rate is 4.6 percent, but our business contacts consistently report little wage pressure.	13	1
201401	1190	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Similarly, our labor union contacts generally see themselves as having little bargaining power in the coming year or two.	13	1
201401	1191	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Why are wage pressures so muted in Minnesota given that the unemployment rate is so low?	13	1
201401	1192	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We certainly don’t have a definitive answer to this question, but it is important to note that, relative to the improvement of the unemployment rate in Minnesota, there has been less improvement in the Minnesota employment-to-population ratio for prime-age people.	13	1
201401	1193	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Also, just as it’s true nationally, the difference between U-6 and U-5 in Minnesota has come down little since its 2009 peak.	13	1
201401	1194	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	When I put all of this information together, I’ve been led to lower my assessment of the wage pressures associated with any unemployment rate.	13	1
201401	1195	2014-01-29	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	There are huge uncertainties, of course, associated with these exercises, but I have lowered my estimate of the long-run mandate- consistent unemployment rate 1 full percentage point over the past year, and, as I said at the beginning of my remarks, at this point I’m basically down to the 5¼ percent that’s in Tealbook A.	13	1

201401	1326	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you, Mr Chairman.	13	1
201401	1327	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Nearly all of the recent economic data have outperformed relative to our December expectations.	13	1
201401	1328	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Most encouraging is the sizable upward revision to real final sales growth in the second half of last year.	13	1
201401	1329	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	This pickup in aggregate demand adds to my confidence in the continued improvement and the underlying momentum of the economy.	13	1
201401	1330	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	My business contacts also are more upbeat—not just the new billionaires from Twitter and those companies.	13	1
201401	1331	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Their growing optimism has translated into solid plans for hiring.	13	1
201401	1332	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Most of my contacts expect to increase their firms’ employment levels this year, a sentiment expressed in broad national surveys of business executives as well.	13	1
201401	1333	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	These hiring plans were especially encouraging because, among the recent data, the one discordant note was struck by last month’s payroll employment release, as a number of people have mentioned.	13	1
201401	1334	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	That one month was likely held down in part by severe weather.	13	1
201401	1335	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Still, over the past several years, lackluster gains in employment relative to population growth have been a concern.	13	1
201401	1336	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, demographic changes are the chief cause for the low employment-to-population ratio, but cyclical factors also play an important role.	13	1
201401	1337	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	One cyclical channel that has gathered some attention is how the drop in house prices and net wealth during the recession made it much more difficult for small entrepreneurs to finance new businesses.	13	1
201401	1338	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Specifically, during the recession and housing bust, employment at new small firms declined much more than at well- established firms.	13	1
201401	1339	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	My staff has looked more closely at how startups have fared during the recovery.	13	1
201401	1340	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	They find that through the early part of the current recovery, startup businesses continued to underperform and show tepid employment growth.	13	1
201401	1341	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Because young, small firms usually account for a substantial share of total employment gains during recoveries, their restraining effect on overall job growth was likely significant.	13	1
201401	1342	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The good news here—the data we’re looking at have a lag, but this does echo some comments by President Fisher—is that with house prices and net wealth having risen so much, financial conditions for entrepreneurs are more favorable than they have been in years, and this should support business formation and, in this regard, provide a tailwind for growth going forward.	13	1
201401	1343	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Turning to inflation, overall and core PCE price measures both increased about 1 percent last year, and inflation appears likely to remain below our target for some time.	13	1
201401	1344	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	As described in the excellent memo by Board staff, the current low rate of inflation can be traced primarily to three factors.	13	1
201401	1345	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	First of all, while the economy is improving, considerable slack remains.	13	1
201401	1346	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Second, import price inflation has been very low, putting downward pressure on domestic prices.	13	1
201401	1347	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	And, third, medical care inflation has fallen, with the medical care CPI posting its smallest December- to-December increase since 1949.	13	1
201401	1348	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	These three factors holding down inflation should diminish over time.	13	1
201401	1349	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Therefore, with inflation expectations remaining well anchored, I expect inflation to start heading back toward our target starting this year.	13	1
201401	1350	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Of course, the Board staff memo also made the point that our understanding of inflation is not very precise.	13	1
201401	1351	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Over the past decade, the largest single contributor to deviations of core inflation from our target is not slack or energy or import price shocks, but the other component, the “kitchen sink” of residual elements.	13	1
201401	1352	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The relative importance of these idiosyncratic factors leads to significant uncertainty and wide confidence intervals around any inflation projection.	13	1
201401	1353	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	One of the key identifiable upside risks to the inflation forecast—that is, one of the known unknowns—centers on the measurement of slack in the economy.	13	1
201401	1354	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	As I have noted before, I view the unemployment rate as a generally reliable measure of slack.	13	1
201401	1355	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	It is consistent with a range of other labor market indicators.	13	1
201401	1356	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	However, I am concerned about how to think about the extraordinary number of long-term unemployed today and their effect on wages and prices.	13	1
201401	1357	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	We have not seen such a situation since the Great Depression, so it is hard to know how to incorporate this factor into our inflation projections.	13	1
201401	1358	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	It may be that the long- and short-term unemployed can be treated as essentially the same for macroeconomic analysis—that is, for measuring slack and forecasting wages and prices.	13	1
201401	1359	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	This is the baseline Tealbook view, and it’s consistent with the US evidence before the recession.	13	1
201401	1360	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	A different interpretation is given by the Tealbook’s alternative view box, which is an innovation I, too, applaud, and I will have my Tealbook signed by Andrew.	13	1
201401	1361	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I have to say, this was incredibly well executed.	13	1
201401	1362	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	It was very thoughtful.	13	1
201401	1363	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	It was fantastic.	13	1
201401	1364	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	And it led to a really good discussion at San Francisco, really kind of honing the arguments.	13	1
201401	1365	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I’m sure that discussion will continue.	13	1
201401	1366	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	In the box, according to this alternative view, a long spell of unemployment sends an adverse signal to employers.	13	1
201401	1367	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The long-term jobless are largely segregated from wage and labor market dynamics.	13	1
201401	1368	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	This has long been the case in Europe, where long-term unemployment has been more prevalent.	13	1
201401	1369	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The European evidence suggests that the long-term unemployed have relatively little influence on wage and price determination.	13	1
201401	1370	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Recent research papers by Bob Gordon and Mark Watson find that the United States may, in fact, be more like Europe in terms of the effects of the long-term unemployed on wages and prices.	13	1
201401	1371	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Specifically, they find evidence that the NAIRU has risen to between 6 and 6½ percent in recent years when it includes all unemployed workers as a measure of slack.	13	1
201401	1372	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	They also find—these are two separate papers and different methodologies—that the NAIRU is relatively stable when one includes only the short-term unemployed, those out of work for less than 27 weeks.	13	1
201401	1373	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	They find a NAIRU for short-term unemployed around 4 percent.	13	1
201401	1374	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, other research finds that, once you include data from the past decade in your sample, only short-term unemployment appears to influence inflation.	13	1
201401	1375	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The long-term unemployed have essentially no influence.	13	1
201401	1376	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	If true, this suggests that US labor markets may not be as immune to hysteresis as many had previously hoped.	13	1
201401	1377	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	It also implies that there is very little slack, at least in terms of the implications for inflation, in the labor market today.	13	1
201401	1378	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	The possible uptrend in unit labor costs noted in the alternative-view box is suggestive in this regard, but it is far from definitive.	13	1
201401	1379	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	But assuming that short-term unemployment is the most relevant metric for slack, inflation will rise more quickly than projected.	13	1
201401	1380	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Indeed, in this case, inflation will likely overshoot our 2 percent target if overall unemployment comes down as anticipated.	13	1
201401	1381	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I’m not arguing that this hypothesis is settled, but if this hypothesis turns out to be right, it has obvious implications for the appropriate setting of monetary policy.	13	1
201401	1382	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I’d just like to add to what President Kocherlakota said.	13	1
201401	1383	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	We should be looking at these alternative measures, whether U-6 or other measures, but I think incorporating them into these kind of statistical models is a useful way to analyze them and maybe evaluate them.	13	1
201401	1384	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	On the other side of the ledger, a downside risk is that we may be underestimating the persistence in the factors that are holding inflation down.	13	1
201401	1385	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	I continue to believe in magic in terms of well-anchored inflation expectations, but, potentially, there are risks that some factors that have held inflation down this year could continue.	13	1
201401	1386	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	One example that I have talked about in the past is medical prices.	13	1
201401	1387	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	They have continued to be soft for some time, as the effects of the Medicare cuts spill over to prices by private insurers.	13	1
201401	1388	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Overall, my outlook for the economy and inflation is quite similar to the Tealbook, and I see balanced risks to these projections.	13	1
201401	1389	2014-01-29	meeting	WILLIAMS	Thank you.	13	1
201401	1390	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Thank you.	13	1

201401	1530	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	All but the nominal income targeting rule point to a positive federal funds rate in the second quarter.	13	1
201401	1531	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	And note that this includes or recognizes the fact that inflation is below target.	13	1
201401	1532	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	This suggests that we are likely going to have to start pulling back on our policy accommodation sooner than we might have once thought.	13	1
201401	1533	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	If so, we need to start thinking about how to best convey that message.	13	1
201401	1534	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	As we approach the 6½ percent threshold, and we continue to try to provide more accommodation through asset purchases, our communications will become more challenging, and we will have to work on that.	13	1
201401	1535	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	The alternative view in Tealbook A, which I applaud and I referenced earlier, makes a plausible case that the economy has nearly recovered from the adverse shock of the Great Recession and has returned to something that looks like steady-state growth.	13	1
201401	1536	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	But even if this is not viewed as the modal forecast, I think it reconciles several conundrums in the data.	13	1
201401	1537	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	For example, there have been several discussions around the table at recent meetings about the declines in the labor force participation rate and concerns that we will see a backup in unemployment once the participation rates return to trend.	13	1
201401	1538	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	The analysis by Shigeru Fujita on our staff that I reported two meetings ago has largely been confirmed and reinforced by the Board staff memo that came out during the intermeeting period.	13	1
201401	1539	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	That is to say that a large portion of the participation rate decline is not cyclical—not all of it, but much of it is noncyclical.	13	1
201401	1540	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	This means that the drop in the unemployment rate, which is much sharper than many anticipated a year ago, remains a pretty good indicator of labor market conditions.	13	1
201401	1541	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	I think it would be prudent for us to think about the implications of the alternative view for our policy choices going forward—a topic that I will return to next go-around.	13	1
201401	1542	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	Before concluding, I’d like to say what an honor it has been, Mr Chairman, to serve on the FOMC with you.	13	1
201401	1543	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	Your leadership has been exemplary through some of the most troubling and challenging times in our nation’s economic history.	13	1
201401	1544	2014-01-29	meeting	PLOSSER	Of course, we will be making official tributes to you tonight at dinner, but I didn’t want to let this opportunity go by without acknowledging my personal deep gratitude for your service and your leadership.	13	1

201401	1621	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	Before getting too excited, though, we have to recognize that transitory factors are responsible for some of the strength in GDP growth we have seen over the past two quarters, as President Lacker noted.	13	1

201401	1625	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	On the inflation side, the data seem generally supportive of the view that inflation will drift up gradually over the next few years toward our 2 percent objective.	13	1

201401	1628	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	On the economic growth side, I expect that the incoming data will provide less of a signal than normal over the next month or two, and I would encourage the staff to do some work on population-weighted heating degree days and how that affects the various economic data.	13	1
201401	1629	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	The winter has been unusually cold, and I really do think this is going to play havoc with the seasonal adjustment process, especially in the construction sector, of course, but I think it also could have meaningful effects elsewhere.	13	1
201401	1630	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	One issue that I had been thinking hard about is the degree of excess slack in the economy.	13	1
201401	1631	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	It seems that the amount of slack in the economy is shrinking faster than we had anticipated given the rate of economic growth.	13	1
201401	1632	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	We have seen the unemployment rate decline quite quickly over the past 18 months, even with a very moderate growth rate.	13	1
201401	1633	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	I am not convinced that the relationship between economic growth and the unemployment rate will necessarily go the other way in 2014—that the unemployment rate will flatten out even as growth stays above trend.	13	1
201401	1634	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	The fact is that we don’t have a good read on three important factors: what productivity growth will be in 2014, so we don’t know how that growth will translate into labor demand; how participation rates will change if the labor market tightens, so we don’t know how a given growth rate of payroll employment will translate to changes in the unemployment rate; and the level of the natural rate.	13	1
201401	1635	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	I think the participation trend, in particular, is very difficult to evaluate.	13	1

201401	1637	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	But parsing out what is temporary from what is permanent is difficult, and this creates considerable uncertainty about the outlook for the unemployment rate and the degree of slack in the labor market.	13	1
201401	1638	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	For me, my uncertainty about how the level of excess slack in the economy will evolve this year, combined with the uncertainty that I have about the growth pace of the economy, makes me pretty uncertain about the ultimate timing of liftoff for short-term rates.	13	1
201401	1639	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	So I really want to put less emphasis on the modal forecast and talk about the degree of dispersion around that forecast.	13	1
201401	1640	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	I can imagine we might want to lift off as soon as the first quarter of next year, or many, many quarters later.	13	1

201401	1642	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	I just think there is too much uncertainty about the timing to continue to formulate it in those terms.	13	1
201401	1643	2014-01-29	meeting	DUDLEY	I think we need to consider how we should adjust the monetary policy statement at the March meeting in light of these considerations.	13	1

201401	1664	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you, Mr Chairman.	13	1
201401	1665	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	I’ve read the limited news that’s rolled in since the December meeting as positive, on net, but there have been some disquieting elements.	13	1
201401	1666	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	Most importantly, economic activity appears to be gradually strengthening.	13	1
201401	1667	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	That’s consistent with my expectation in the December SEP and with our Committee’s assessment when we decided to undertake our first measured reduction in the pace of asset purchases.	13	1
201401	1668	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	We’ve not yet seen clear signs that inflation is moving back gradually toward our 2 percent objective, but it would be unrealistic to expect such signs to emerge in just a month or so of data.	13	1

201401	1670	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	I’ve read a fair bit of discussion among the staff and outside analysts concerning the potential effects of bad weather, but I’ve not seen anyone make the case that this factor could explain the entire shortfall relative to expectations of more than 100,000.	13	1
201401	1671	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	In spite of the weak December payroll report and thanks, in part, to upward revisions to November’s gains, the three-month moving average stands around 170,000, a level that clearly is consistent with ongoing improvement in the labor market.	13	1
201401	1672	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	For now, my hope is that the December jobs reading will prove to be a temporary aberration and that payrolls, on average, will post stronger gains going forward.	13	1
201401	1673	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	The household survey was more mixed, and I see the main question there as whether to interpret the large decline in the unemployment rate as signaling an equally large reduction in the degree of labor market slack.	13	1
201401	1674	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	This assessment is made more difficult by the accompanying 0.2 percentage point decline in labor force participation.	13	1
201401	1675	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	We’ve debated any number of times over the past year or so whether labor market slack is now becoming increasingly manifest in lower labor force participation rather than in measured unemployment, and there are arguments on both sides of this debate.	13	1
201401	1676	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	It’s important for us to refine our thinking on this matter because, after declining about 30 basis points per year, from 2010 through 2012, the labor force participation rate declined a whopping 84 basis points last year.	13	1
201401	1677	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	I found the staff memo on the behavior of the participation rate helpful in thinking through various potential explanations.	13	1
201401	1678	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	Concerning the role of demographics, the memo rightly highlights the fact that the shift in cohort sizes toward older cohorts with lower participation rates dominates the modest increase in participation rates of older workers.	13	1
201401	1679	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	But we are confronting a difficult identification problem, because these demographic changes coincide with the aftermath of a particularly deep recession.	13	1
201401	1680	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	It’s conceivable that the demographic changes imply a steeper decline in the trend rate of labor force participation than the 0.3 percentage point per year assumed in the Tealbook baseline, but I remain reasonably optimistic that a substantial fraction of the decline in participation among younger cohorts would be reversed in a strong economy with a higher pressure job market.	13	1
201401	1681	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	In particular, I’m not at all convinced that many among the younger and prime-age cohorts who currently state they do not want a job would give the same answer if the job market were notably stronger, if, for example, quits and the pace of hiring were closer to normal levels and wages were showing more substantial gains.	13	1
201401	1682	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	While I’m inclined to discount at least a portion of the large decline in the unemployment rate in December, it does seem fair to conclude that the drop signals at least some reduction in slack.	13	1
201401	1683	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	This view seems consistent with job market perceptions in recent surveys and the news we received on growth.	13	1
201401	1684	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	The level of real GDP in the fourth quarter is now projected to be 0.6 percent higher than we thought at the time of the December meeting.	13	1
201401	1685	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	Some of this upward surprise is due to stronger net exports, which are unlikely to continue making large contributions to economic growth going forward, but private domestic final purchases accounted for about one-half of the upward revision to GDP.	13	1
201401	1686	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	The strength we’ve seen in this component is certainly good news, and it suggests that the long-awaited acceleration in household and business spending may have finally arrived.	13	1
201401	1687	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	Although the risks to this outlook now seem reasonably balanced, the recent volatility in financial conditions faced by emerging markets bears careful watching.	13	1
201401	1688	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	But, to date, it appears unlikely to derail the generally more positive outlook for the global economy.	13	1
201401	1689	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	While I am hopeful that we’re finally breaking out of the 2 percent growth pattern, I think we should be careful not to get carried away with our progress.	13	1
201401	1690	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	Let’s keep in mind that we’ve experienced quite a few false dawns over the past few years, and even if faster growth is now for real, the absolute level of slack in the economy is probably still quite large by historical standards.	13	1
201401	1691	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	The unemployment rate provides an incomplete measure.	13	1
201401	1692	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	In addition to the difficult- to-estimate cyclical shortfall in labor force participation, our assessment of slack needs to factor in the unusually large portion of the labor force that is underemployed.	13	1
201401	1693	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	The fraction of the labor force working part time for economic reasons still stands at 5 percent, a level not far below its post-recession peak of 6 percent.	13	1
201401	1694	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	Moreover, at 2½ percent, long-term unemployment still stands far above its pre-recession level of 1 percent.	13	1
201401	1695	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	Furthermore, inflation has been disconcertingly low over the past year, and, at this stage, reversion of inflation toward our 2 percent objective remains only a forecast.	13	1
201401	1696	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	We noted in our statement that low inflation could pose risks to the economy.	13	1
201401	1697	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	I think a persistent downside miss could also pose risks to our credibility.	13	1
201401	1698	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	On balance, at this juncture, with the unemployment rate falling rapidly, I see our main challenge is finding ways to communicate that we intend to keep policy highly accommodative for quite some time and that we’ll be patient in removing accommodation when the time comes to do so.	13	1
201401	1699	2014-01-29	meeting	YELLEN	I, too, Mr Chairman, want to express my gratitude for all of your contributions to the Federal Reserve and to the nation and say what an honor it has been to serve with you.	13	1

201401	1702	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Governor Tarullo.	13	1
201401	1703	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Mr Chairman.	13	1
201401	1704	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	When the go-round got to me at the December meeting, I characterized what I had heard to that point as, generally, people having not changed their baseline or modal expectations very much, but having seen a reduction in downside risks.	13	1
201401	1705	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	This time around, up until about the past 15 minutes, I would have characterized those who have gone before me as, again, not seeing much of a change in modal expectations but having had some increase or some sense of perhaps increased upside risks.	13	1
201401	1706	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Beginning with Jeff, though, I think there’s been a little bit more of a line that captures some of what I’m feeling, which is at least a little bit more doubt around that.	13	1
201401	1707	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	A lot of the good news to which people referred was backward looking.	13	1
201401	1708	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	The upping of the second half of 2013’s economic performance was sort of:  It’s good, we had it, but, particularly as Jeff said with things like inventory buildup, there’s not actually a particular suggestion that that’s going to carry through to more momentum.	13	1
201401	1709	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	If anything, it might be slightly less.	13	1
201401	1710	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Taking note of the trio of less-than-uplifting pieces of data that people have mentioned that we’ve seen over the past couple of weeks—the jobs report, housing permits, and durable goods orders—two of those three are forward looking, and the fact that two of the three that are forward looking are not so great gives me at least a little bit of a pause.	13	1
201401	1711	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	The subjective points for optimism, which are people and businesses reporting that they feel better about things, we should probably take note of, but, of course, those are susceptible to a turnaround as well when things in the world aren’t going as positively as expected.	13	1
201401	1712	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	In that regard, I just want to mention a couple of things about external risk—which is to say, the possibility of financial turmoil in emerging markets.	13	1
201401	1713	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	I say external, even though one factor is presumably the actual and anticipated rise in US rates with the presumed effects of reversing some of the capital flows to emerging markets that resulted from the search for higher returns over the past several years.	13	1
201401	1714	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	I don’t know how great this effect is, but because emerging markets’ financial problems over the past few decades do seem to be, at least generally, correlated with interest rate movements in larger, mature economies, I suspect there is some causal relationship with our monetary policy, as well as with the pullback from some emerging markets by many European banks as they took risk off their balance sheets largely based on non- European asset sales.	13	1
201401	1715	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	I’m inclined toward the view that Steve and others have already articulated, that the current stress in a number of emerging markets is not likely either to develop into a full-blown and widespread crisis or to have substantial direct effects on the US economy.	13	1
201401	1716	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Important background factors in many important emerging markets have changed for the better since the 1990s, including fewer hard pegs of exchange rates, considerably higher reserve levels, and a greater proportion of debt denominated in local currencies.	13	1
201401	1717	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	But there is at least one other background change—namely, the exponential increase in capital flows to emerging markets over the past decade or so—that may make a crisis more likely because there’s a lot more that could run.	13	1
201401	1718	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	I’m a little less than totally reassured by some of the grounds given by a number of commentators for believing that the problems will remain muted.	13	1
201401	1719	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Foremost among these is the differentiation point—that is, the notion that Turkey, Argentina, and Ukraine, which are among the most affected economies, are very different and have significant and special issues that don’t apply to the others.	13	1
201401	1720	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	As Steve has already mentioned, in the summer of 1997, Thailand looked a lot different from Indonesia, Korea, and other countries to many observers both within and without the US government.	13	1
201401	1721	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	What seemed in mid-1997 to be problems in these countries in need of attention, but manageable, suddenly became serious vulnerabilities.	13	1
201401	1722	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Eventually investors did draw distinctions among the affected countries, but in the presence of a lot of uncertainty and fear, they tended to run first in a quite undifferentiated fashion in response to developments that didn’t seem logically sufficient to change people’s perceptions of those economies that much.	13	1
201401	1723	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	This is the impact of something approaching a herd mentality or behavior in the absence of enough information.	13	1
201401	1724	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	I’ll just quickly mention some other things that are a little bit worrisome about the current circumstances.	13	1
201401	1725	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Several of those countries—Turkey, Argentina, and Ukraine—are also good examples of countries that have really quite bad relationships with the IMF right now for different reasons.	13	1
201401	1726	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Argentina has a 14-year history now of bad relationships.	13	1
201401	1727	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Turkey had a lot of interaction with the IMF for quite a while, but in recent years that’s become strained.	13	1
201401	1728	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	And of course, Ukraine has just got substantial political problems that stop it from having very coherent policies more generally.	13	1
201401	1729	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	That’s important because obviously it makes it less likely that problems that develop in those economies can be reasonably contained with a Fund program if there’s a bad basis on which to build the Fund program in the first place.	13	1
201401	1730	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	That leads me to another observation, which I suspect is related to the point about bad relations with the IMF.	13	1
201401	1731	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	In conversations with officials, colleagues, counterparts from some of the emerging markets, I sense more worry in them than I might have anticipated based on the kind of analysis that we’ve been giving one another today and in the past few days.	13	1
201401	1732	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	When you’re on the firing line, of course, things always seem a little bit more dramatic, and so you tend to be a little bit more worried, but some of the countries that people would put in the middle category of vulnerability seem extremely focused on what can be done to move Argentina, Turkey, and some of the other countries in a more stabilizing direction.	13	1
201401	1733	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	A final point of difference is that the United States and other mature economies have way less room to provide some buffering or absorption capacity than we did in either 1994–95 or 1997–98.	13	1
201401	1734	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	I mean, people will remember that a turning point of the 1997–98 emerging market crisis came, I think, when our predecessors here reduced the federal funds rate in non-regularly scheduled meetings very significantly in the course of just a couple of weeks.	13	1
201401	1735	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	We’re obviously not in a position to do that.	13	1
201401	1736	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Now, I’m aware there are a lot of experts out there who have predicted eight out of the last two emerging market financial crises, and, as I said, my guess is that this episode will remain largely contained, but I think the downside risk is actually somewhat greater than has been generally recognized.	13	1
201401	1737	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Then, briefly, switching gears for a second, I also wanted to say something about the labor markets.	13	1
201401	1738	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	First, I wanted to make one point on the memo, which I agree was very good, although I cannot keep the five hypotheses straight in my mind.	13	1
201401	1739	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	People have been noting that the memo attributes most of the change in labor force participation to structural factors.	13	1
201401	1740	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	That’s correct but a little bit misleading, given our policy decision, because, pre-crisis, the expectation of labor economists was for a declining labor force participation rate because of demographic reasons, the aging of the population.	13	1
201401	1741	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Really, from our point of view, the key should be explaining the delta between what that projection in, say, 2007 or 2008 would have been and what we’re seeing today.	13	1
201401	1742	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	And I think, when you look at it that way, you probably see a more balanced explanation between structural and cyclical factors.	13	1
201401	1743	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	After several years of very high unemployment and insufficient aggregate demand as well as, perhaps, an acceleration of some preexisting unfavorable secular tendencies, the case for structural effects on the labor market is unfortunately becoming stronger, and the absence of aggressive active labor market policies has almost certainly exacerbated whatever is, in fact, occurring.	13	1
201401	1744	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	But as Charlie Evans and Narayana did, I think it’s useful to focus a bit on what we have seen with respect to wages, because here I don’t think that we’re seeing a lot of evidence that whatever slack does exist is being reduced so rapidly that we’re at the limits of what can be productively done.	13	1
201401	1745	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Some research notes by outside organizations have suggested that wage pressures are building.	13	1
201401	1746	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	These notes have emphasized the year-to-year percentage change in average hourly earnings for production and nonsupervisory workers.	13	1
201401	1747	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	It’s true that this measure of wages has accelerated over the past 18 months or so.	13	1
201401	1748	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Through December, the average hourly earnings series rose 2.1 percent from a year ago, which was a noticeable pickup from what was, after all, a historically low 1¼ percent pace in late 2012.	13	1
201401	1749	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	But the average hourly earnings of production workers is pretty much the only wage series that shows evidence of marked acceleration.	13	1
201401	1750	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Rates of increase in average hourly earnings for all workers and in the employment cost index for private workers are basically unchanged since 2010, with not much evidence of recent acceleration.	13	1
201401	1751	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Compensation per hour for workers in the nonfarm business sector presents a fuzzier picture, but, from what I understand, that series is noisy enough that it’s difficult to draw much inference from the recent movements that we’ve seen.	13	1
201401	1752	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Anecdotal reports notwithstanding, it’s hard to find much evidence of industry-specific wage pressures by looking at aggregate data.	13	1
201401	1753	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	Wage gains have been pretty muted across a variety of industries, including those in the service sector.	13	1
201401	1754	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	There are a couple of industries, several of you have already mentioned residential construction, in which there do seem to be more impressive wage gains, but there are nearly always some discrete sectors in which wages are rising well above the norm, and these are not at all necessarily harbingers of more generalized tendencies.	13	1
201401	1755	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	If we’re talking about construction workers, it’s probably worth noting that for the construction sector as a whole—that is, not just residential but nonresidential construction—wages have continued, on net, to decelerate since the end of the recession.	13	1
201401	1756	2014-01-29	meeting	TARULLO	So although I think the questions, as many of you have already noted, about what’s going on in labor markets remain open and contestable and certainly very important for our purposes, I don’t think that, at this point, the case has yet been made for structural explanations dominating cyclical ones to such an extent that it should be affecting near- to medium-term monetary policies.	13	1

201401	1783	2014-01-29	meeting	STEIN	Different people think about things differently.	13	1
201401	1784	2014-01-29	meeting	STEIN	Note that once we get below an unemployment rate of 6.5 percent, we’ve said qualitative things, but they’re qualitative, and because they’re not completely unambiguous, there has been some mix of economic contingency and time dependence.	13	1

201401	1830	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	I guess there’s a cautionary tale in there somewhere, which is probably that when we know that initial reports of incoming data come wrapped in a label that says “Caution: Very high standard errors,” it really does pay to let policy carry forward with some inertia.	13	1
201401	1831	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	On that theory, I do think it’s wise, for the time being, to look past the December employment report, the market turmoil, and other signs of weakness.	13	1
201401	1832	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	Bill Dudley may have a point that the weather has been so awful that we could be looking at a few weeks of people not going to restaurants, not going to movies, certainly not looking at houses, and things like that.	13	1
201401	1833	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	Still, I’m left with the thought that the case for 3 percent or better economic growth in 2014 overall feels much more plausible now on the back of the strength that the economy has shown.	13	1
201401	1834	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	It also seems wise and conservative to me to allow the medium-term forecast to remain not too much changed despite a 3¾ percent second half of the year.	13	1
201401	1835	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	David Wilcox and others have mentioned some of the factors—stronger dollar, higher rates, and others—that cause us to want to hold back and certainly not increase that.	13	1
201401	1836	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	I think the wise thing is to wait and see.	13	1
201401	1837	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	With that, I want to briefly comment on rate policy going forward and, really, the issues that we face as a Committee.	13	1
201401	1838	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	The Committee is going to be discussing enhancing forward guidance in March, it sounds like, because there’s a good chance that we’ll pass through the unemployment threshold in the very near future.	13	1
201401	1839	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	In thinking about what to tell the market in the near term, it seems to me that some more-fundamental questions arise.	13	1
201401	1840	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	The continuing downward adjustments to aggregate supply and the alternative view box underscore that there is real uncertainty about how close the economy is to potential output.	13	1
201401	1841	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	There are plenty of credible people out there who are discussing and arguing this.	13	1
201401	1842	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	John Williams mentioned a couple.	13	1
201401	1843	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	There was some work along the same lines done by members of the Council of Economic Advisers last summer that they shared with us.	13	1
201401	1844	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	One question that arises, and I think this comes through very well in the inflation memo, is, how would we know if we were closer to potential output than we think?	13	1
201401	1845	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	Let’s say that the economy performs about as expected, but the economy is significantly closer to potential than the baseline suggests.	13	1
201401	1846	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	Will inflation start to send signals to us, or have disinflationary global forces or well-grounded expectations semipermanently muffled the inflation signal?	13	1
201401	1847	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	The whole framework for analyzing wage and price increases seems to me to be in question, and so I wonder how confident one can be that inflation would actually respond as predicted if that were the case.	13	1
201401	1848	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	In a way it’s a two-sided problem.	13	1
201401	1849	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	We not only don’t know exactly how close we are to potential.	13	1
201401	1850	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	We also don’t know whether inflation will react as predicted in the textbooks.	13	1
201401	1851	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	At that point, would asset prices step in to send a signal?	13	1
201401	1852	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	That may very well happen.	13	1
201401	1853	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	I don’t know that that signal would tell us anything particularly about potential output, although there’s very interesting staff work on that question going on.	13	1
201401	1854	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	But independent of the question of how close we are to potential, it is very possible that keeping rates low for another year can provide too much support for asset prices.	13	1
201401	1855	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	We have, after all, been very happy to take credit for strong housing and equity prices, appropriately so, and I don’t see any reason to be shy now about the ability of highly accommodative monetary policy to drive prices much, much higher.	13	1
201401	1856	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	I also don’t see why highly accommodative monetary policy, particularly if we do have stronger economic growth, couldn’t produce a damaging asset bubble well before the economy reaches full potential.	13	1
201401	1857	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	Of course, I don’t argue that we observe that.	13	1
201401	1858	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	We clearly don’t observe that on a broad basis, although it is an interesting fact that, as the Monday morning staff briefing showed, spreads for single-B and triple-C bonds have recently approached 15-year lows, which is to say extremely low, lower even than they were in the pre-financial-crisis bubble.	13	1
201401	1859	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	The question of how should the Committee react to a world of solid economic growth, low inflation, but high and rising asset prices, presents itself as a possibility—again, not a forecast.	13	1
201401	1860	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	What should the Committee be saying in its public communications about these things beginning, perhaps, in March?	13	1
201401	1861	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	To me, the danger of financial-stability risks comes now as the recovery strengthens, and I don’t know that the current guidance adequately addresses that.	13	1
201401	1862	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	Let me also freely admit that the fact that the inflation signal may be broken, it is also broken on the downside.	13	1
201401	1863	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	What makes it so hard is that it may not be sending a signal we’d love for it to send about increasing slack or a higher level of slack.	13	1
201401	1864	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	In any case, it’s great to have the first, and perhaps the second, taper done.	13	1
201401	1865	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	This next round of issues are going to be no less challenging, and I look forward to our discussions.	13	1
201401	1866	2014-01-29	meeting	POWELL	I want to close, Mr Chairman, by thanking you for your extraordinary leadership of this institution and for the great honor of serving with you.	13	1

201401	1878	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	I thought I would start with a quick summary of yesterday’s discussion and add a few comments, and then we can turn to the policy go-round.	13	1
201401	1879	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Incoming data have generally been positive, showing strong second-half economic growth.	13	1
201401	1880	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Many saw growth as likely to continue at or above trend.	13	1
201401	1881	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	On the other hand, inventory investment and a surge in net exports accounted for some of the recent growth and may not persist, and we have seen false dawns before.	13	1
201401	1882	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Although considerable uncertainty remains, risks appear more balanced with respect to economic growth and employment.	13	1
201401	1883	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	In the household sector, consumption spending picked up in the latter part of 2013 and should be well supported going forward by growing employment, wages, and net worth.	13	1
201401	1884	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Housing activity continues to be solid in those Districts that reported, with prices moving higher.	13	1
201401	1885	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Commercial real estate is also picking up in some Districts.	13	1
201401	1886	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	The December payroll report was surprising and may have been an anomaly or the result of bad weather.	13	1
201401	1887	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Overall, employment conditions appear to be improving, with surveys more optimistic.	13	1
201401	1888	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Participants continued to debate the usefulness of the unemployment rate as a measure of labor market slack.	13	1
201401	1889	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	On the one hand, declines in participation and increases in underemployment and long-term unemployment suggest that labor slack is greater than the unemployment rate indicates.	13	1
201401	1890	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Indeed, low rates of inflation in prices and wages are consistent with slack putting downward pressure on labor costs.	13	1
201401	1891	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	On the other hand, it may be that many of the long-term unemployed and those who have left the labor force did not have the skills needed by employers and, thus, are effectively structurally unemployed.	13	1
201401	1892	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	In this case, we may already be near effective full employment.	13	1
201401	1893	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	One factor slowing employment growth has been the absence of startups.	13	1
201401	1894	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	This may be changing as increases in net worth facilitate entrepreneurship.	13	1
201401	1895	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Some business contacts were tentatively bullish, seeing higher growth in the medium term.	13	1
201401	1896	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	There were more reports of increased cap-ex and hiring or plans to hire.	13	1
201401	1897	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Declining policy uncertainty may also be helping.	13	1
201401	1898	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	However, some contacts reported that hiring was constrained by the lack of workers with appropriate skills.	13	1
201401	1899	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Strong activity was reported in a number of industries, including railroads and shipping, airlines, tourism, and marijuana growing.	13	1
201401	1900	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Manufacturing indicators are positive.	13	1
201401	1901	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	There were few references this time to fiscal conditions, although one participant noted that some fiscal drag remains.	13	1
201401	1902	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Recent turmoil in emerging markets does not yet appear to be a threat to the US economy, although it bears watching, as contagion can be hard to predict.	13	1
201401	1903	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Another concern is a potential downgrade for Puerto Rico.	13	1
201401	1904	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	In the banking sector, deposit growth is outstripping loan growth, with the difference being made up by reserves.	13	1
201401	1905	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Banks do generally see credit metrics improving, however, and more customers are interested in expansion.	13	1
201401	1906	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Inflation remains below target, though relatively stable at that level.	13	1
201401	1907	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Firms don’t have much pricing power, and cost pressures are low and expected to remain low, with a few exceptions, such as the construction industry.	13	1
201401	1908	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Some transitory factors, including low growth in import prices and low rates of health-care inflation, are playing some role in holding inflation down.	13	1
201401	1909	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Overall, most participants expect inflation to move gradually back to target because of the gravitational pull of well-anchored inflation expectations.	13	1
201401	1910	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	However, the heavy reliance on the stability of inflation expectations is somewhat worrisome, in that we don’t have a good understanding of how expectations themselves are determined.	13	1
201401	1911	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	On the policy front, upcoming possible challenges include managing the forward guidance as the economy strengthens and we pass through the 6.5 percent unemployment threshold, as well as dealing with possible increases in term premiums or bubbles in asset prices even as unemployment remains high and inflation low.	13	1
201401	1912	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	That’s a quick summary.	13	1

201401	1937	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	The labor market discussion was very interesting.	13	1
201401	1938	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Without going into all of the details, let me just draw a quantitative conclusion from the discussion in the box in Tealbook A, about the possibility that we are now already close to full employment.	13	1
201401	1939	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	That perspective says, basically, that the long-term unemployed and those out of the labor force are no longer effectively putting any pressure on wages, for example.	13	1
201401	1940	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	A consequence of that, just to put some numbers on it, is that if you add together the number of long-term unemployed with those who are not in the labor force but who say they want a job now—so we are excluding the retired, the disabled, and people currently in school—that comes to about 10 million people.	13	1
201401	1941	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Those same two categories added up to 6 million at the peak of the business cycle, so there are about 4 million additional people now who say they want to be working and, by this theory, are structurally unemployed.	13	1
201401	1942	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	That’s about 2.7 percent of the labor force of 150 million.	13	1
201401	1943	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	In addition, the number of people who are working part time for economic reasons has risen from 4.6 million in 2007 to 7.8 million—that’s another 3 million people.	13	1
201401	1944	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	So, roughly speaking, an implication of this Tealbook box story is that in the past seven years, somewhere between 2½ and 5 percent of the labor force has become structurally unemployed, which, if true, is a tragedy, of course, as we have lost a huge amount of labor resources.	13	1
201401	1945	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Now, it seems implausible to me that that number of people would become structurally unemployed because of skills mismatch and changes in industrial composition, et cetera, in such a period of time.	13	1
201401	1946	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	I don’t think that that’s what’s happening.	13	1
201401	1947	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	If there is a case for the Tealbook box, it is that the length of time out of work has led to atrophy of skills and lack of labor-force attachment, and that’s what’s causing the problem, in which case I think that from a policy point of view this cuts both ways.	13	1
201401	1948	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	On the one hand, if these people really are irredeemable, then there’s not much we can do about it, as sad as it may be.	13	1
201401	1949	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	On the other hand, if the issue really is giving them a chance to get back into work and reacquire experience and skills, that would actually be an argument for more forceful policy.	13	1
201401	1950	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	The main point I want to draw here is that this is a very important debate.	13	1
201401	1951	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	It does have very important quantitative implications for the labor force and for the future of our economy.	13	1

201401	1960	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	And then, finally, there is the pattern we have begun, though we may not continue it, of measured reductions in asset purchases.	13	1
201401	1961	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Given our statement that we will not raise rates until a considerable period after that program has ended, that is also creating an implication of somewhere in 2015.	13	1
201401	1962	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	So we have a number of pillars in this guidance at this point, which I think is what’s keeping it pretty well tied down.	13	1

201401	2026	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	However, I would note that the fact that we are not pausing despite a weak labor market report, recent correlated stock market declines globally, and persistently low inflation rates implies a very high hurdle for pausing on the path of very gradual reductions in the purchase program.	23	2
201401	2027	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	But if there is a very high hurdle for stopping or slowing the taper, there should then be a very high hurdle for tapering more quickly.	23	2

201401	2032	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	In essence, my concern is about risk management.	23	2
201401	2033	2014-01-29	meeting	ROSENGREN	In light of the difficulties in separating trend from cycle in the data and the potential for new shocks emanating from outside the United States, we should be quite sure that the economy is on much stronger footing and that the inflation rate is clearly returning toward target before altering our current path of very gradual changes in our purchase program.	23	2

201401	2153	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	I think the key issue for the Committee, as outlined yesterday in our remarks about the economy, is whether this is a false dawn or not.	23	2
201401	2154	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Obviously we’ve had a lot of stops and starts during the past several years.	23	2
201401	2155	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	I thought President Lacker did a good job of outlining the case that some of the economic growth we’ve seen might be temporary.	23	2
201401	2156	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	If it is a false dawn, I think the Committee is actually well positioned.	23	2
201401	2157	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	This is the mindset that we’ve had in designing current policy.	23	2
201401	2158	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	We have a leisurely tapering baseline, I would say.	23	2
201401	2159	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	We have expectations of the first increase of the policy rate far in the future, and we have a tapering program that’s state contingent, so we can alter it if necessary.	23	2
201401	2160	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	So I think we’re well positioned in the case of economic growth slowing from the pace that it hit in the second half of 2013.	23	2
201401	2161	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	I’m going to concentrate on the other case—What if this is not a false dawn, if it’s the real thing?—and follow up on some of Governor Stein’s comments yesterday and President Plosser’s comments earlier this morning.	23	2
201401	2162	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	I think we’re less well positioned for this case.	23	2
201401	2163	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Probably our best response in this case would be to increase the pace of tapering, but this would, in effect, pull up the entire time line of the first policy rate increase.	23	2
201401	2164	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	That would be okay if it was warranted by stronger data, but that’s what we would be doing in that situation.	23	2
201401	2165	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Let’s suppose we do not face a false dawn.	23	2
201401	2166	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Instead we face the real thing, a faster economy that is on a sustainable growth path.	23	2
201401	2167	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	My main comment is going to be to outline my version of a key challenge for the Committee in this scenario in 2014, which is the possible emergence of the idea that the FOMC is “behind the curve.”	23	2
201401	2168	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	President Plosser has noted yesterday and today that our suite of Taylor-type rules now suggests that the policy rate should be rising off zero either now or in the near future.	23	2
201401	2169	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	I’m going to take that as a key fact because I take these Taylor-type rules to describe normal FOMC behavior as characterized by the research literature.	23	2
201401	2170	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	So let’s divide the period of a near-zero policy rate into two parts.	23	2
201401	2171	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	The first part is the one in which the actual policy rate is near zero and Taylor-type rules also recommend zero.	23	2
201401	2172	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	This is the world that we’ve been in over the past several years, and this is what we’re used to thinking about recently.	23	2
201401	2173	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	And then there’s a different world, in which the actual policy rate is near zero, but the Taylor-type rules recommend more than zero, which is what we’re entering into now.	23	2
201401	2174	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	In previous discussions around the table here, I’ve called this second part the “Woodford period.”	23	2
201401	2175	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	We’re now entering this Woodford period, and I see it as a very challenging period for the Committee.	23	2
201401	2176	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	During the pre-Woodford period, we made promises to keep the policy rate lower for longer.	23	2
201401	2177	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	One interpretation of those promises, and maybe the one I favor, is that they did not represent a credible commitment by this Committee to keep rates lower for longer.	23	2
201401	2178	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Instead, these promises were interpreted by financial markets as representing a pessimistic outlook for medium-term macroeconomic prospects in the United States.	23	2
201401	2179	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Financial markets expected rates to remain low, indeed, but only because the economy was expected to remain weak.	23	2
201401	2180	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	This created an equilibrium of sorts between the Committee and financial markets.	23	2
201401	2181	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	We’ve been in this equilibrium—a word I use loosely here—for some time.	23	2
201401	2182	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Now we’re going to move into the Woodford period, and such an interpretation by financial markets will no longer be possible.	23	2
201401	2183	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Taylor-type rules representing so-called normal Fed behavior will call for a higher policy rate than what’s observed.	23	2
201401	2184	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	The Committee intends to keep the policy rate low.	23	2
201401	2185	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	The Committee will, exactly at this point—that is, in 2014—begin to gain credibility for its lower-for-longer policy.	23	2
201401	2186	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	This may, in fact, feed a boom in the US economy.	23	2
201401	2187	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	The way this is supposed to work in the Woodford model is that you made the promise a long time ago, three years ago, and you got a boom three years ago because you made that promise.	23	2
201401	2188	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	I’m arguing that that promise wasn’t credible.	23	2
201401	2189	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	It was interpreted as a pessimistic outlook for the US economy.	23	2
201401	2190	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	But now, when Taylor rules are diverging from what we’re actually doing with the policy rate, financial markets are going to say, “Well, they really do mean to stay lower for longer,” and the boom will come today instead of when it was supposed to come three years ago.	23	2
201401	2191	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Financial markets’ interpretation of what we’re doing is going to have to change in 2014.	23	2
201401	2192	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Likely what they will do is argue that the FOMC is “behind the curve,” which is a typical thing that occurs in financial markets, and they’ll just point to simple evidence that the Committee isn’t following Taylor-type rules that are calling for higher policy rates.	23	2
201401	2193	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	So it’s going to sound like past episodes in which the FOMC has lagged behind where markets thought it should be.	23	2
201401	2194	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	I think this is possibly a major challenge for the Committee in 2014.	23	2
201401	2195	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	The Committee will need to have a story to counteract this.	23	2
201401	2196	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	There are simple stories that can be told.	23	2
201401	2197	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	You can lean against the economic data.	23	2
201401	2198	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	You can argue that the economic data aren’t as strong as they appear to be, that the economy is actually weaker than the markets think it is, and you can have that debate with financial markets.	23	2
201401	2199	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	That’s fine, but that gets difficult at some point if the data really are coming in stronger than anticipated.	23	2
201401	2200	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	There’s only so far you can go in making that kind of argument.	23	2
201401	2201	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Another argument that we could make is that this is a Woodford follow-through.	23	2
201401	2202	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	We made a commitment to “lower for longer.”	23	2
201401	2203	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	That means that the reason we’re keeping policy rates low today is to make up for the period when we were constrained by the zero bound.	23	2
201401	2204	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	I do not see enough consensus on the Committee to make that argument, but that is one argument that could be made.	23	2
201401	2205	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	So I don’t think we really have a good plan right now about how we’re going to handle this in 2014, and we need to think about this.	23	2
201401	2206	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	We need to hone our message during coming meetings to handle this possibility.	23	2
201401	2207	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Of course, this is all under the scenario in which economic growth is actually faster and sustainably faster, not under a scenario in which the economy slows down again.	23	2
201401	2208	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	By the way, I think low inflation is helpful.	23	2
201401	2209	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	It does give us some cover, and that’s a big advantage for us, but I would remind everybody that inflation is actually included in the Taylor- type rules.	23	2
201401	2210	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	So the fact that those rules are calling for liftoff already incorporates the fact that inflation is somewhat away from our target on the low side.	23	2
201401	2211	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	I just wanted to reiterate that point in a way that I hope was more coherent than I usually am.	23	2
201401	2212	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	And for today, Mr Chairman, I support option B as written.	23	2
201401	2213	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	I do think we’re on a reasonable course for now, and we’ll await more information to see whether the false dawn scenario yet again haunts us.	23	2
201401	2214	2014-01-29	meeting	BULLARD	Thank you.	23	2

201401	2310	2014-01-29	meeting	GEORGE	With a combined 50 basis point swing, it could be argued that policy is now more accommodative than at the time of the previous meeting.	23	2
201401	2311	2014-01-29	meeting	GEORGE	Of course, I understand that considerable uncertainty surrounds such estimates, but those kinds of shifts seem relevant to me in terms of assessing the future stance of policy.	23	2
201401	2312	2014-01-29	meeting	GEORGE	Changes in the estimate of excess capacity also are relevant to current and future policy.	23	2
201401	2313	2014-01-29	meeting	GEORGE	While economic growth did surprise to the upside in the second half of this year, potential GDP at the end of 2013 was revised down by about 2 percent over the past year.	23	2
201401	2314	2014-01-29	meeting	GEORGE	With the output gap estimated to be 1.9 percent at the end of 2014, a similar revision to potential GDP would put the output gap close to zero at the end of this year.	23	2
201401	2315	2014-01-29	meeting	GEORGE	I’m not necessarily expecting that the Tealbook estimate of potential will be revised lower, but my point is that, given the trajectory of labor force participation and the expiration of emergency unemployment compensation, I see such a revision as a risk, and a concerning risk, given that rates are likely to still be at zero at the end of this year.	23	2
201401	2316	2014-01-29	meeting	GEORGE	In the meantime, our policy settings continue to facilitate incentives that pose potential risks to long-term financial stability.	23	2
201401	2317	2014-01-29	meeting	GEORGE	Asset prices and froth in various pockets of the financial sector deserve our ongoing monitoring, particularly in high-yield bond issuance and leveraged lending.	23	2
201401	2318	2014-01-29	meeting	GEORGE	I recognize that identifying these risks to financial stability and quantifying their impact today remain difficult for sure, but we should not underestimate their potential effects or their ability to surprise.	23	2
201401	2319	2014-01-29	meeting	GEORGE	Finally, Mr Chairman, I want to join others in wishing you all the best and thanking you for your service.	23	2
201401	2320	2014-01-29	meeting	BERNANKE	Thank you.	23	2

201401	2337	2014-01-29	meeting	LACKER	Knowing how much, over the eight years we have served together, you must have enjoyed my policy lectures—I mean, statements [laughter]—I gave thought, in honor of your final meeting, to reviewing and recapitulating the highlights of my recommendations and commentary about how to conduct monetary policy.	23	2
